**[Large Strategic Science Missions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Large_Strategic_Science_Missions "Large Strategic Science Missions")**

_Astrophysics Division_

The **Hubble Space Telescope** (**HST** or **Hubble**) is a [space telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_telescope "Space telescope") that was launched into [low Earth orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit "Low Earth orbit") in 1990 and remains in operation. It was not the [first space telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbiting_Solar_Observatory "Orbiting Solar Observatory"), but it is one of the largest and most versatile, renowned as a vital research tool and as a public relations boon for [astronomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomy "Astronomy"). The Hubble Space Telescope is named after astronomer [Edwin Hubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edwin_Hubble "Edwin Hubble") and is one of [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA")'s [Great Observatories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Observatories_program "Great Observatories program"). The [Space Telescope Science Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Telescope_Science_Institute "Space Telescope Science Institute") (STScI) selects Hubble's targets and processes the resulting data, while the [Goddard Space Flight Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center "Goddard Space Flight Center") (GSFC) controls the spacecraft.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-8)

Hubble features a 2.4 m (7 ft 10 in) mirror, and its five main instruments observe in the [ultraviolet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultraviolet "Ultraviolet"), [visible](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visible_spectrum "Visible spectrum"), and [near-infrared](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near-infrared "Near-infrared") regions of the [electromagnetic spectrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum "Electromagnetic spectrum"). Hubble's orbit outside the distortion of [Earth's atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth "Atmosphere of Earth") allows it to capture extremely high-resolution images with substantially lower background light than ground-based telescopes. It has recorded some of the most detailed visible light images, allowing a deep view into space. Many Hubble observations have led to breakthroughs in [astrophysics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrophysics "Astrophysics"), such as [determining the rate of expansion of the universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble%27s_law#Measurements_of_the_Hubble_constant "Hubble's law").

The Hubble Space Telescope was funded and built in the 1970s by NASA with contributions from the [European Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Agency "European Space Agency"). Its intended launch was in 1983, but the project was beset by technical delays, budget problems, and the 1986 [_Challenger_ disaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster "Space Shuttle Challenger disaster"). Hubble was launched on [STS-31](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-31 "STS-31") in 1990, but its main mirror had been ground incorrectly, resulting in [spherical aberration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spherical_aberration "Spherical aberration") that compromised the telescope's capabilities. The optics were corrected to their intended quality by a servicing mission, [STS-61](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-61 "STS-61"), in 1993.

Hubble is the only telescope designed to be maintained in space by astronauts. Five Space Shuttle missions repaired, upgraded, and replaced systems on the telescope, including all five of the main instruments. The [fifth mission](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-125 "STS-125") was initially canceled on safety grounds following the [_Columbia_ disaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster "Space Shuttle Columbia disaster") (2003), but after [NASA administrator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Administrator_of_NASA "Administrator of NASA")[Michael D. Griffin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_D._Griffin "Michael D. Griffin") approved it, the servicing mission was completed in 2009. Hubble completed 30 years of operation in April 2020[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-hubb-site-30-anniv-1) and is predicted to last until 2030 to 2040.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-cbsnews20130530-4)

Hubble is the visible light telescope in NASA's [Great Observatories program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Observatories_program "Great Observatories program"); other parts of the spectrum are covered by the [Compton Gamma Ray Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Compton_Gamma_Ray_Observatory "Compton Gamma Ray Observatory"), the [Chandra X-ray Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory "Chandra X-ray Observatory"), and the [Spitzer Space Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope "Spitzer Space Telescope") (which covers the infrared bands).[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-9) The mid-IR-to-visible band successor to the Hubble telescope is the [James Webb Space Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Webb_Space_Telescope "James Webb Space Telescope") (JWST), which was launched on December 25, 2021, with the [Nancy Grace Roman Space Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nancy_Grace_Roman_Space_Telescope "Nancy Grace Roman Space Telescope") due to follow in 2027.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-NASA-20200716-10)[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-NYT-20200716-11)[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-12)

### Proposals and precursors

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Proposals and precursors")]

In 1923 [Hermann Oberth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermann_Oberth "Hermann Oberth"), considered a father of modern rocketry along with [Robert H. Goddard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_H._Goddard "Robert H. Goddard") and [Konstantin Tsiolkovsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konstantin_Tsiolkovsky "Konstantin Tsiolkovsky"), published _\_[Die Rakete zu den Planetenräumen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Die\\_Rakete\\_zu\\_den\\_Planetenr%C3%A4umen "Die Rakete zu den Planetenräumen")\__ ("The Rocket into Planetary Space"), which mentioned how a telescope could be propelled into [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth "Earth") orbit by a rocket.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-13)

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/1946-_Lyman_Spitzer_%284526166350%29.jpg/250px-1946-_Lyman_Spitzer_%284526166350%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1946-_Lyman_Spitzer_(4526166350).jpg)

[Lyman Spitzer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lyman_Spitzer "Lyman Spitzer") played a major role in the birth of the Hubble Space Telescope project.

The history of the Hubble Space Telescope can be traced to 1946, to [astronomer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomer "Astronomer")[Lyman Spitzer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lyman_Spitzer "Lyman Spitzer")'s paper "Astronomical advantages of an extraterrestrial observatory".[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-:0-14) In it, he discussed the two main advantages that a space-based observatory would have over ground-based telescopes. First, the [angular resolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_resolution "Angular resolution") (the smallest separation at which objects can be clearly distinguished) would be limited only by [diffraction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diffraction-limited_system "Diffraction-limited system"), rather than by the turbulence in the atmosphere, which causes stars to twinkle, known to astronomers as [seeing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_seeing "Astronomical seeing"). At that time ground-based telescopes were limited to resolutions of 0.5–1.0 [arcseconds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arcsecond "Arcsecond"), compared to a theoretical diffraction-limited resolution of about 0.05 arcsec for an optical telescope with a [mirror](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mirror "Mirror") 2.5 m (8 ft 2 in) in diameter. Second, a space-based telescope could observe [infrared](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Infrared "Infrared") and ultraviolet light, which are strongly absorbed by the [atmosphere of Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth "Atmosphere of Earth").[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-:0-14)

Spitzer devoted much of his career to pushing for the development of a space telescope.[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-15) In 1962, a report by the U.S. [National Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences "National Academy of Sciences") recommended development of a [space telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_telescope "Space telescope") as part of the [space program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_spaceflight "Human spaceflight"), and in 1965, Spitzer was appointed as head of a committee given the task of defining scientific objectives for a large space telescope.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-16)

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Nancy_Grace_Roman_with_Space_Telescope_Model_in_1966_%2827154772837%29.jpg/250px-Nancy_Grace_Roman_with_Space_Telescope_Model_in_1966_%2827154772837%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Nancy_Grace_Roman_with_Space_Telescope_Model_in_1966_(27154772837).jpg)

[Nancy Grace Roman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nancy_Grace_Roman "Nancy Grace Roman") with a model of the Large Space Telescope that was eventually developed as the Hubble Space Telescope. While listed as a 1966 photo, this design was not the standard until the mid-1970s.

Also crucial was the work of [Nancy Grace Roman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nancy_Roman "Nancy Roman"), the "Mother of Hubble".[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-17) Well before it became an official [NASA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NASA "NASA") project, she gave public lectures touting the scientific value of the telescope. After it was approved, she became the program scientist, setting up the steering committee in charge of making astronomer needs feasible to implement[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-18) and writing testimony to [Congress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Congress "United States Congress") throughout the 1970s to advocate continued funding of the telescope.[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-AnnRev-19) Her work as project scientist helped set the standards for NASA's operation of large scientific projects.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-WilliamsBook-20)

[![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Studio_portrait_photograph_of_Edwin_Powell_Hubble_%28cropped%29.JPG/250px-Studio_portrait_photograph_of_Edwin_Powell_Hubble_%28cropped%29.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Studio_portrait_photograph_of_Edwin_Powell_Hubble_(cropped).JPG)

American astronomer [Edwin P. Hubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edwin_P._Hubble "Edwin P. Hubble") (pictured in 1931), for which the telescope is named.

Space-based astronomy had begun on a very small scale following [World War II](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_War_II "World War II"), as scientists made use of developments that had taken place in [rocket](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rocket "Rocket") technology. The first ultraviolet [spectrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum "Electromagnetic spectrum") of the [Sun](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun "Sun") was obtained in 1946,[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-21) and NASA launched the [Orbiting Solar Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbiting_Solar_Observatory "Orbiting Solar Observatory") (OSO) to obtain UV, X-ray, and gamma-ray spectra in 1962.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-22) An [orbiting solar telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ariel_1 "Ariel 1") was launched in 1962 by the United Kingdom as part of the [Ariel programme](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ariel_programme "Ariel programme"), and in 1966 NASA launched the first [Orbiting Astronomical Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbiting_Astronomical_Observatory "Orbiting Astronomical Observatory") (OAO) mission. OAO-1's battery failed after three days, terminating the mission. It was followed by [Orbiting Astronomical Observatory 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbiting_Astronomical_Observatory_2 "Orbiting Astronomical Observatory 2") (OAO-2), which carried out ultraviolet observations of [stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star "Star") and [galaxies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy "Galaxy") from its launch in 1968 until 1972, well beyond its original planned lifetime of one year.[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-23)

The OSO and OAO missions demonstrated the important role space-based observations could play in astronomy. In 1968, NASA developed firm plans for a space-based [reflecting telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reflecting_telescope "Reflecting telescope") with a mirror 3 m (9.8 ft) in diameter, known provisionally as the Large Orbiting Telescope or Large Space Telescope (LST), with a launch slated for 1979. These plans emphasized the need for crewed maintenance missions to the telescope to ensure such a costly program had a lengthy working life, and the concurrent development of plans for the reusable [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle") indicated that the technology to allow this was soon to become available.[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitzer197932-24)

The continuing success of the OAO program encouraged increasingly strong consensus within the astronomical community that the LST should be a major goal. In 1970, NASA established two committees, one to plan the engineering side of the space telescope project, and the other to determine the scientific goals of the mission. Once these had been established, the next hurdle for NASA was to obtain funding for the instrument, which would be far more costly than any Earth-based telescope. The [U.S. Congress](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Congress "United States Congress") questioned many aspects of the proposed budget for the telescope and forced cuts in the budget for the planning stages, which at the time consisted of very detailed studies of potential instruments and hardware for the telescope. In 1974, [public spending](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Public_spending "Public spending") cuts led to Congress deleting all funding for the telescope project.[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitzer197933%E2%80%9334-25)

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg/250px-Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Astronaut_Owen_Garriott_Performs_EVA_During_Skylab_3_-_GPN-2002-000065.jpg)

Astronaut [Owen Garriott](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Owen_Garriott "Owen Garriott") working next to Skylab's crewed solar space observatory, 1973

In 1977, then NASA Administrator [James C. Fletcher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_C._Fletcher "James C. Fletcher") proposed a token $5 million for Hubble in NASA's budget. Then NASA Associate Administrator for Space Science, [Noel Hinners](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noel_Hinners "Noel Hinners"), instead cut all funding for Hubble, gambling that this would galvanize the scientific community into fighting for full funding. As Hinners recalls:[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-26)

> It was clear that year that we weren't going to be able to get a full-up start. There was some opposition on [Capitol] Hill to getting a new start on [Hubble]. It was driven, in large part as I recall, by the budget situation. Jim Fletcher proposed that we put in five million as a placeholder. I didn't like that idea. It was, in today's vernacular, a "sop" to the astronomy community. "There's something in there, so all is well".
> 
> 
> I figured in my own little head that to get that community energized we'd be better off zeroing it out. Then they would say, "Whoa, we're in deep trouble", and it would marshal the troops. So I advocated that we not put anything in. I don't remember any of the detailed discussions or whether there were any, but Jim went along with that so we zeroed it out. It had, from my perspective, the desired impact of stimulating the astronomy community to renew their efforts on the lobbying front. While I like to think in hindsight it was a brilliant political move, I'm not sure I thought it through all that well. It was something that was spur of the moment.
> 
> 
> [...] five million would let them think that all is well anyway, but it's not. So let's give them a message. My own thinking, get them stimulated to get into action. Zeroing it out would certainly give that message. I think it was as simple as that. Didn't talk to anybody else about doing it first, just, "Let's go do that". Voila, it worked. Don't know whether I'd do that again.

The political ploy worked. In response to Hubble being zeroed out of NASA's budget, a nationwide lobbying effort was coordinated among astronomers. Many astronomers met [congressmen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives "United States House of Representatives") and [senators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Senate "United States Senate") in person, and large-scale letter-writing campaigns were organized. The [National Academy of Sciences](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Academy_of_Sciences "National Academy of Sciences") published a report emphasizing the need for a space telescope, and eventually, the Senate agreed to half the budget that had originally been approved by Congress.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitzer197934-27)

The funding issues led to a reduction in the scale of the project, with the proposed mirror diameter reduced from 3 m to 2.4 m, both to cut costs[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-gander-28) and to allow a more compact and effective configuration for the telescope hardware. A proposed precursor 1.5 m (4 ft 11 in) space telescope to test the systems to be used on the main satellite was dropped, and budgetary concerns also prompted collaboration with the European Space Agency (ESA). ESA agreed to provide funding and supply one of the first generation instruments for the telescope, as well as the [solar cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_cell "Solar cell") that would power it, and staff to work on the telescope in the United States, in return for European astronomers being guaranteed at least 15% of the observing time on the telescope.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-29) Congress eventually approved funding of $36 million for 1978, and the design of the LST began in earnest, aiming for a launch date of 1983.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTESpitzer197934-27) In 1983, the telescope was named after [Edwin Hubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edwin_Hubble "Edwin Hubble"),[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-30) who confirmed one of the greatest scientific discoveries of the 20th century, made by [Georges Lemaître](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georges_Lema%C3%AEtre "Georges Lemaître"), that the [universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe "Universe") is [expanding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expanding_universe "Expanding universe").[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-31)

### Construction and engineering

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Construction and engineering")]

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Hubble_mirror_polishing.jpg/250px-Hubble_mirror_polishing.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hubble_mirror_polishing.jpg)

Grinding of Hubble's primary mirror at Perkin-Elmer, March 1979

Once the Space Telescope project had been given the go-ahead, work on the program was divided among many institutions. [Marshall Space Flight Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center "Marshall Space Flight Center") (MSFC) was given responsibility for the design, development, and construction of the telescope, while [Goddard Space Flight Center](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goddard_Space_Flight_Center "Goddard Space Flight Center") was given overall control of the scientific instruments and ground-control center for the mission.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunarWaring1999487%E2%80%93488-32) MSFC commissioned the optics company [Perkin-Elmer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PerkinElmer "PerkinElmer") to design and build the [optical telescope assembly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_train "Optical train") (OTA) and Fine Guidance Sensors for the space telescope. [Lockheed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lockheed_Corporation "Lockheed Corporation") was commissioned to construct and integrate the spacecraft in which the telescope would be housed.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunarWaring1999489-33)

### Optical telescope assembly

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Optical telescope assembly")]

Optically, the HST is a [Cassegrain reflector](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cassegrain_reflector "Cassegrain reflector") of [Ritchey–Chrétien design](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ritchey%E2%80%93Chr%C3%A9tien_telescope "Ritchey–Chrétien telescope"), as are most large professional telescopes. This design, with two hyperbolic mirrors, is known for good imaging performance over a wide field of view, with the disadvantage that the mirrors have shapes that are hard to fabricate and test. The mirror and optical systems of the telescope determine the final performance, and they were designed to exacting specifications. Optical telescopes typically have mirrors polished to an [accuracy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accuracy "Accuracy") of about a tenth of the [wavelength](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wavelength "Wavelength") of [visible light](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visible_light "Visible light"), but the Space Telescope was to be used for observations from the visible through the ultraviolet (shorter wavelengths) and was specified to be [diffraction limited](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diffraction-limited_system "Diffraction-limited system") to take full advantage of the space environment. Therefore, its mirror needed to be polished to an accuracy of 10 nanometers, or about 1/65 of the wavelength of red light.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-ScienceSPF-34) On the long wavelength end, the OTA was not designed with optimum infrared performance in mind, e.g. the mirrors are kept at stable (and warm, about 15°C) temperatures by heaters. This limits Hubble's performance as an infrared telescope.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-IR-35)

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/A20010288000_NASM2017-10014_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-A20010288000_NASM2017-10014_%28cropped%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:A20010288000_NASM2017-10014_(cropped).jpg)

The backup mirror, by Kodak; its inner support structure can be seen because it is not coated with a reflective surface.

Perkin-Elmer (PE) intended to use custom-built and extremely sophisticated [computer-controlled polishing machines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer-aided_manufacturing "Computer-aided manufacturing") to grind the mirror to the required shape.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunarWaring1999489-33) However, in case their cutting-edge technology ran into difficulties, NASA demanded that PE sub-contract to [Kodak](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kodak "Kodak") to construct a back-up mirror using traditional mirror-polishing techniques.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllenAngelMangusRodney19903%E2%80%934-36) (The team of Kodak and [Itek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Itek "Itek") also bid on the original mirror polishing work. Their bid called for the two companies to double-check each other's work, which would have almost certainly caught the polishing error that later caused [problems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#Flawed_mirror).)[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-37) The Kodak mirror is now on permanent display at the [National Air and Space Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum "National Air and Space Museum").[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-38)[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-39) An Itek mirror built as part of the effort is now used in the 2.4 m telescope at the [Magdalena Ridge Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magdalena_Ridge_Observatory "Magdalena Ridge Observatory").[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-40)

Construction of the Perkin-Elmer mirror began in 1979, starting with a blank manufactured by [Corning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corning_Incorporated "Corning Incorporated") from their ultra-low expansion glass. To keep the mirror's weight to a minimum it consisted of top and bottom plates, each 25 mm (0.98 in) thick, sandwiching a [honeycomb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Honeycomb "Honeycomb") lattice. Perkin-Elmer simulated [microgravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microgravity "Microgravity") by supporting the mirror from the back with 130 rods that exerted varying amounts of force.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-41) This ensured the mirror's final shape would be correct and to specification when deployed. Mirror polishing continued until May 1981. NASA reports at the time questioned Perkin-Elmer's managerial structure, and the polishing began to slip behind schedule and over budget. To save money, NASA halted work on the back-up mirror and moved the launch date of the telescope to October 1984.[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunarWaring1999496-42) The mirror was completed by the end of 1981; it was washed using 9,100 L (2,000 imp gal; 2,400 US gal) of hot, [deionized water](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deionized_water "Deionized water") and then received a reflective coating of 65‑nm‑thick [aluminum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aluminium "Aluminium") and a protective coating of 25‑nm‑thick [magnesium fluoride](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnesium_fluoride "Magnesium fluoride").[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-IR-35)[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-43)

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Early_stages_of_Hubble_construction.jpg/250px-Early_stages_of_Hubble_construction.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Early_stages_of_Hubble_construction.jpg)

The OTA, metering truss, and secondary baffle are visible in this image of Hubble during early construction.

Doubts continued to be expressed about Perkin-Elmer's competence on a project of this importance, as their budget and timescale for producing the rest of the OTA continued to inflate. In response to a schedule described as "unsettled and changing daily", NASA postponed the launch date of the telescope until April 1985. Perkin-Elmer's schedules continued to slip at a rate of about one month per quarter, and at times delays reached one day for each day of work. NASA was forced to postpone the launch date until March and then September 1986. By this time, the total project budget had risen to $1.175 billion.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunarWaring1999504-44)

The spacecraft in which the telescope and instruments were to be housed was another major engineering challenge. It would have to withstand frequent passages from direct sunlight into the darkness of Earth's [shadow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shadow "Shadow"), which would cause major changes in temperature, while being stable enough to allow extremely accurate pointing of the telescope. A shroud of [multi-layer insulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multi-layer_insulation "Multi-layer insulation") keeps the temperature within the telescope stable and surrounds a light aluminum shell in which the telescope and instruments sit. Within the shell, a [graphite-epoxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_fiber_reinforced_polymer "Carbon fiber reinforced polymer") frame keeps the working parts of the telescope firmly aligned.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-45) Because graphite composites are [hygroscopic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hygroscopic "Hygroscopic"), there was a risk that water vapor absorbed by the truss while in Lockheed's clean room would later be expressed in the vacuum of space; resulting in the telescope's instruments being covered by ice. To reduce that risk, a nitrogen gas purge was performed before launching the telescope into space.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-46)

As well as electrical power systems, the **Pointing Control System** controls HST orientation using five types of sensors (magnetic sensors, optical sensors, and six gyroscopes) and two types of [actuators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Actuator "Actuator") ([reaction wheels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reaction_wheel "Reaction wheel") and [magnetic torquers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_torquer "Magnetic torquer")).[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-PCS-47)

While construction of the spacecraft in which the telescope and instruments would be housed proceeded somewhat more smoothly than the construction of the OTA, Lockheed experienced some budget and schedule slippage, and by the summer 1985, construction of the spacecraft was 30% over budget and three months behind schedule. An MSFC report said Lockheed tended to rely on NASA directions rather than take their own initiative in the construction.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunarWaring1999508-48)

### Computer systems and data processing

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=7 "Edit section: Computer systems and data processing")]

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/DF-224.jpg/330px-DF-224.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:DF-224.jpg)

DF-224 in Hubble, before it was replaced in 1999

The two initial, primary computers on the HST were the 1.25 [MHz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hertz "Hertz")[DF-224](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DF-224 "DF-224") system, built by Rockwell Autonetics, which contained three redundant CPUs, and two redundant [NSSC-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSSC-1 "NSSC-1") (NASA Standard Spacecraft Computer, Model 1) systems, developed by [Westinghouse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Westinghouse_Electric_Corporation "Westinghouse Electric Corporation") and GSFC using [diode–transistor logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diode%E2%80%93transistor_logic "Diode–transistor logic") (DTL). A co-processor for the DF-224 was added during Servicing Mission 1 in 1993, which consisted of two redundant strings of an Intel-based 80386 processor with an 80387 math co-processor.[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-49) The DF-224 and its 386 co-processor were replaced by a 25 MHz Intel-based 80486 processor system during [Servicing Mission 3A](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#Servicing_Mission_3A) in 1999.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-50) The new computer is 20 times faster, with six times more memory, than the [DF-224](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DF-224 "DF-224") it replaced. It increases throughput by moving some computing tasks from the ground to the spacecraft and saves money by allowing the use of modern programming languages.[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-51)

Additionally, some of the science instruments and components had their own embedded microprocessor-based control systems. The MATs (Multiple Access Transponder) components, MAT-1 and MAT-2, use Hughes Aircraft CDP1802CD microprocessors.[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-52) The [Wide Field and Planetary Camera](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera "Wide Field and Planetary Camera") (WFPC) also used an [RCA 1802 microprocessor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/RCA_1802 "RCA 1802") (or possibly the older 1801 version).[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-53) The WFPC-1 was replaced by the [WFPC-2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera_2 "Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2") during Servicing Mission 1 in 1993, which was then replaced by the [Wide Field Camera 3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_Field_Camera_3 "Wide Field Camera 3") (WFC3) during Servicing Mission 4 in 2009. The upgrade extended Hubble's capability of seeing deeper into the universe and providing images in three broad regions of the spectrum.[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-54)[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-55)

### Initial instruments

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=8 "Edit section: Initial instruments")]

[![Image 11](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/HubbleExploded_edit_1.svg/500px-HubbleExploded_edit_1.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:HubbleExploded_edit_1.svg)

[Exploded view](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploded_view "Exploded view") of the Hubble Space Telescope

When launched, the HST carried five scientific instruments: the Wide Field and Planetary Camera (WF/PC), Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph (GHRS), High Speed Photometer (HSP), Faint Object Camera (FOC) and the Faint Object Spectrograph (FOS). WF/PC used a radial instrument bay, and the other four instruments were each installed in an axial instrument bay.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-CP-2244-56)

WF/PC was a high-resolution imaging device primarily intended for optical observations. It was built by NASA's [Jet Propulsion Laboratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory "Jet Propulsion Laboratory"), and incorporated a set of 48 [filters](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Filter_(optics) "Filter (optics)") isolating [spectral lines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectral_line "Spectral line") of particular astrophysical interest. The instrument contained eight [charge-coupled device](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charge-coupled_device "Charge-coupled device") (CCD) chips divided between two cameras, each using four CCDs. Each CCD has a resolution of 0.64 megapixels.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-wfpc2-57) The wide field camera (WFC) covered a large angular field at the expense of resolution, while the planetary camera (PC) took images at a longer effective [focal length](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Focal_length "Focal length") than the WF chips, giving it a greater magnification.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-CP-2244-56)

The [Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goddard_High_Resolution_Spectrograph "Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph") (GHRS) was a [spectrograph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectrograph "Spectrograph") designed to operate in the ultraviolet. It was built by the Goddard Space Flight Center and could achieve a [spectral resolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectral_resolution "Spectral resolution") of 90,000.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-58) Also optimized for ultraviolet observations were the FOC and FOS, which were capable of the highest spatial resolution of any instruments on Hubble. Rather than CCDs, these three instruments used [photon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photon "Photon")-counting [digicons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digicon "Digicon") as their detectors. The FOC was constructed by ESA, while the [University of California, San Diego](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_California,_San_Diego "University of California, San Diego"), and [Martin Marietta Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Marietta_Corporation "Martin Marietta Corporation") built the FOS.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-CP-2244-56)

The final instrument was the HSP, designed and built at the [University of Wisconsin–Madison](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison "University of Wisconsin–Madison"). It was optimized for visible and ultraviolet light observations of [variable stars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Variable_star "Variable star") and other astronomical objects varying in brightness. It could take up to 100,000 measurements per second with a [photometric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photometry_(astronomy) "Photometry (astronomy)") accuracy of about 2% or better.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-59)

HST's guidance system can also be used as a scientific instrument. Its three [Fine Guidance Sensors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fine_guidance_sensor "Fine guidance sensor") (FGS) are primarily used to keep the telescope accurately pointed during an observation, but can also be used to carry out extremely accurate [astrometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrometry "Astrometry"); measurements accurate to within 0.0003 arcseconds have been achieved.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-60)

[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Hubble_Control_Centre.jpg/250px-Hubble_Control_Centre.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hubble_Control_Centre.jpg)

Hubble Control Center at Goddard Space Flight Center, 1999

The Space Telescope Science Institute (STScI) is responsible for the scientific operation of the telescope and the delivery of data products to astronomers. STScI is operated by the [Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Association_of_Universities_for_Research_in_Astronomy "Association of Universities for Research in Astronomy") (AURA) and is physically located in [Baltimore, Maryland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baltimore,_Maryland "Baltimore, Maryland") on the Homewood campus of [Johns Hopkins University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University "Johns Hopkins University"), one of the 39 U.S. universities and seven international affiliates that make up the AURA consortium. STScI was established in 1981[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Edmondson1997-61)[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-62) after something of a power struggle between NASA and the scientific community at large. NASA had wanted to keep this function in-house, but scientists wanted it to be based in an [academic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Academia "Academia") establishment.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunarWaring1999486%E2%80%93487-63)[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-64) The [Space Telescope European Coordinating Facility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Telescope_European_Coordinating_Facility "Space Telescope European Coordinating Facility") (ST-ECF), established at [Garching bei München](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Garching_bei_M%C3%BCnchen "Garching bei München") near [Munich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Munich "Munich") in 1984, provided similar support for European astronomers until 2011, when these activities were moved to the European Space Astronomy Centre.[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-65)

One complex task that falls to STScI is scheduling observations for the telescope.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Team_Hubble-66) Hubble is in a low-Earth orbit to enable servicing missions, which results in most astronomical targets being [occulted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occultation "Occultation") by the Earth for slightly less than half of each orbit. Observations cannot take place when the telescope passes through the [South Atlantic Anomaly](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Atlantic_Anomaly "South Atlantic Anomaly") due to elevated [radiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiation "Radiation") levels, and there are also sizable exclusion zones around the Sun (precluding observations of [Mercury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercury_(planet) "Mercury (planet)")), Moon and Earth. The solar avoidance angle is about 50°, to keep sunlight from illuminating any part of the OTA. Earth and Moon avoidance keeps bright light out of the FGSs, and keeps scattered light from entering the instruments. If the FGSs are turned off, the Moon and Earth can be observed. Earth observations were used very early in the program to generate flat-fields for the WFPC1 instrument. There is a so-called continuous viewing zone (CVZ), within roughly 24° of Hubble's [orbital poles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_pole "Orbital pole"), in which targets are not [occulted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occultation "Occultation") for long periods.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-67)[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-68)[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-69)

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Diagram_of_Hubble%27s_orbit.jpg/250px-Diagram_of_Hubble%27s_orbit.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Diagram_of_Hubble%27s_orbit.jpg)

Hubble's low orbit means many targets are visible for slightly more than half of an orbit's elapsed time, since they are blocked from view by the [Earth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth "Earth") for almost one-half of each orbit.

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Animation_of_Hubble_Space_Telescope_trajectory.gif/250px-Animation_of_Hubble_Space_Telescope_trajectory.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Animation_of_Hubble_Space_Telescope_trajectory.gif)

Animation of Hubble's orbit from October 31, 2018, to December 25, 2018; Earth is not shown.

Due to the [precession](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Precession "Precession") of the orbit, the location of the CVZ moves slowly over a period of eight weeks. Because the [limb](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/limb#Etymology_2 "wikt:limb") of the Earth is always within about 30° of regions within the CVZ, the brightness of scattered [earthshine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earthshine "Earthshine") may be elevated for long periods during CVZ observations. Hubble orbits in low Earth orbit at an altitude of approximately 540 kilometers (340 mi) and an inclination of 28.5°.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-heavens-above-5) The position along its orbit changes over time in a way that is not accurately predictable. The density of the upper atmosphere varies according to many factors, and this means Hubble's predicted position for six weeks' time could be in error by up to 4,000 km (2,500 mi). Observation schedules are typically finalized only a few days in advance, as a longer lead time would mean there was a chance the target would be unobservable by the time it was due to be observed.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrolgerRose201746-70) Engineering support for HST is provided by NASA and contractor personnel at the Goddard Space Flight Center in [Greenbelt, Maryland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greenbelt,_Maryland "Greenbelt, Maryland"), 48 km (30 mi) south of the STScI. Hubble's operation is monitored 24 hours per day by four teams of flight controllers who make up Hubble's Flight Operations Team.[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Team_Hubble-66)

### _Challenger_ disaster, delays, and eventual launch

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=10 "Edit section: Challenger disaster, delays, and eventual launch")]

[![Image 15](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/STS-31_Hubble_launch_roll_and_pitch.jpg/250px-STS-31_Hubble_launch_roll_and_pitch.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:STS-31_Hubble_launch_roll_and_pitch.jpg)

[STS-31](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-31 "STS-31") lifts off, carrying Hubble into orbit.

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/1990_s31_IMAX_view_of_HST_release.jpg/250px-1990_s31_IMAX_view_of_HST_release.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1990_s31_IMAX_view_of_HST_release.jpg)

Hubble being deployed from _Discovery_ in 1990

By January 1986, the planned launch date for Hubble that October looked feasible, but the [_Challenger_ disaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Challenger_disaster "Space Shuttle Challenger disaster") brought the U.S. space program to a halt, grounded the Shuttle fleet, and forced the launch to be postponed for several years. During this delay the telescope was kept in a clean room, powered up and purged with nitrogen, until a launch could be rescheduled. This costly situation (about $6 million per month) pushed the overall costs of the project higher. However, this delay allowed time for engineers to perform extensive tests, swap out a possibly failure-prone battery, and make other improvements.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTETatarewicz1998371-71) Furthermore, the ground software needed to control Hubble was not ready in 1986, and was barely ready by the 1990 launch.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-72) Following the resumption of shuttle flights, [Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle")[_Discovery_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Discovery "Space Shuttle Discovery") successfully launched the Hubble on April 24, 1990, as part of the STS-31 mission.[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-73)

At launch, NASA had spent approximately $4.7 billion in inflation-adjusted 2010 dollars on the project.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-74) Hubble's cumulative costs are estimated to be about $11.3 billion in 2015 dollars, which include all subsequent servicing costs, but not ongoing operations, making it the most expensive science mission in NASA history.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-75)

List of Hubble instruments
--------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: List of Hubble instruments")]

Hubble accommodates five science instruments at a given time, plus the [Fine Guidance Sensors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fine_Guidance_Sensor "Fine Guidance Sensor"), which are mainly used for aiming the telescope but are occasionally used for scientific [astrometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrometry "Astrometry") measurements. Early instruments were replaced with more advanced ones during the Shuttle servicing missions. COSTAR was a corrective optics device rather than a science instrument, but occupied one of the four axial instrument bays.

Since the final servicing mission in 2009, the four active instruments have been ACS, COS, STIS and WFC3. NICMOS is kept in hibernation, but may be revived if WFC3 were to fail in the future.

*   [Advanced Camera for Surveys](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Advanced_Camera_for_Surveys "Advanced Camera for Surveys") (ACS; 2002–present)
*   [Cosmic Origins Spectrograph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_Origins_Spectrograph "Cosmic Origins Spectrograph") (COS; 2009–present)
*   [Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corrective_Optics_Space_Telescope_Axial_Replacement "Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement") (COSTAR; 1993–2009)
*   [Faint Object Camera](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faint_Object_Camera "Faint Object Camera") (FOC; 1990–2002)
*   [Faint Object Spectrograph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faint_Object_Spectrograph "Faint Object Spectrograph") (FOS; 1990–1997)
*   [Fine Guidance Sensor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fine_Guidance_Sensor "Fine Guidance Sensor") (FGS; 1990–present)
*   [Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goddard_High_Resolution_Spectrograph "Goddard High Resolution Spectrograph") (GHRS/HRS; 1990–1997)
*   [High Speed Photometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High_Speed_Photometer "High Speed Photometer") (HSP; 1990–1993)
*   [Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near_Infrared_Camera_and_Multi-Object_Spectrometer "Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer") (NICMOS; 1997–present, hibernating since 2008)
*   [Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Telescope_Imaging_Spectrograph "Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph") (STIS; 1997–present (non-operative 2004–2009))
*   [Wide Field and Planetary Camera](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera "Wide Field and Planetary Camera") (WFPC; 1990–1993)
*   [Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera_2 "Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2") (WFPC2; 1993–2009)
*   [Wide Field Camera 3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_Field_Camera_3 "Wide Field Camera 3") (WFC3; 2009–present)

Of the former instruments, three (COSTAR, FOS and WFPC2) are displayed in the Smithsonian [National Air and Space Museum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Air_and_Space_Museum "National Air and Space Museum").[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-76)[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-:2-77)[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-78) The FOC is in the [Dornier](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dornier_Flugzeugwerke "Dornier Flugzeugwerke") museum, Germany.[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-79) The HSP is in the Space Place at the [University of Wisconsin–Madison](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Wisconsin%E2%80%93Madison "University of Wisconsin–Madison").[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-80) The first WFPC was dismantled, and some components were then re-used in WFC3.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-81)[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-82)

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hubble_PSF_with_flawed_optics.jpg/250px-Hubble_PSF_with_flawed_optics.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hubble_PSF_with_flawed_optics.jpg)

An extract from a WF/PC image shows the light from a star spread over a wide area instead of being concentrated on a few pixels.

Within weeks of the launch of the telescope, the returned images indicated a serious problem with the optical system. Although the first images appeared to be sharper than those of ground-based telescopes, Hubble failed to achieve a final sharp focus and the best image quality obtained was drastically lower than expected. Images of [point sources](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point_source "Point source") spread out over a radius of more than one arcsecond, instead of having a [point spread function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point_spread_function "Point spread function") (PSF) concentrated within a circle 0.1[arcseconds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arcsecond "Arcsecond") (485 n[rad](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radian "Radian")) in diameter, as had been specified in the design criteria.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-83)[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-84)

Analysis of the flawed images revealed that the primary mirror had been polished to the wrong shape. Although it was believed to be one of the most precisely [figured](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Figuring "Figuring") optical mirrors ever made, smooth to about 10 nanometers,[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-ScienceSPF-34) the outer perimeter was too flat by about 2200 nanometers (about 1⁄450 mm or 1⁄11000 inch).[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Servicing_Mission_1-85) This difference was catastrophic, introducing severe spherical aberration, a flaw in which light reflecting off the edge of a mirror [focuses](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Focus_(optics) "Focus (optics)") on a different point from the light reflecting off its center.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTETatarewicz1998375-86)

The effect of the mirror flaw on scientific observations depended on the particular observation. The core of the aberrated PSF was sharp enough to permit high-resolution observations of bright objects, and spectroscopy of point sources was affected only through a sensitivity loss; however, the loss of light to the large, out-of-focus halo severely reduced the usefulness of the telescope for faint objects or high-contrast imaging. This meant nearly all the cosmological programs were essentially impossible, since they required observation of exceptionally faint objects.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTETatarewicz1998375-86) This led politicians to question NASA's competence, scientists to rue the cost which could have gone to more productive endeavors, and comedians to make jokes about NASA and the telescope. In the 1991 comedy _[The Naked Gun 2½: The Smell of Fear](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Naked\_Gun\_2%C2%BD:\_The\_Smell\_of\_Fear "The Naked Gun 2½: The Smell of Fear")_, in a scene where historical disasters are displayed, Hubble is pictured with the _[Titanic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Titanic "Titanic")_ and the [_Hindenburg_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hindenburg_disaster "Hindenburg disaster").[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-87)[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTETatarewicz1998373-88) Nonetheless, during the first three years of the Hubble mission, before the optical corrections, the telescope carried out a large number of productive observations of less demanding targets.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-89) The error was well characterized and stable, enabling astronomers to partially compensate for the defective mirror by using sophisticated [image processing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Image_processing "Image processing") techniques such as [deconvolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deconvolution "Deconvolution").[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunarWaring1999514%E2%80%93515-90)

### Origin of the problem

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=13 "Edit section: Origin of the problem")]

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/PIA22913-HubbleSpaceTelescope-ComparisonOfCameraImages-20181204.jpg/500px-PIA22913-HubbleSpaceTelescope-ComparisonOfCameraImages-20181204.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:PIA22913-HubbleSpaceTelescope-ComparisonOfCameraImages-20181204.jpg)

Spiral galaxy [M100](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Messier_100 "Messier 100") as seen before corrective optics (left) and after

A commission headed by [Lew Allen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lew_Allen "Lew Allen"), director of the [Jet Propulsion Laboratory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jet_Propulsion_Laboratory "Jet Propulsion Laboratory"), was established to determine how the error could have arisen. The Allen Commission found that a reflective [null corrector](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Null_corrector "Null corrector"), a testing device used to achieve a properly shaped non-spherical mirror, had been incorrectly assembled. (One lens was out of position by 1.3 mm (0.051 in).)[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllenAngelMangusRodney19907-1-91) During the initial grinding and polishing of the mirror, [Perkin-Elmer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perkin-Elmer "Perkin-Elmer") analyzed its surface with two conventional refractive null correctors. However, for the final manufacturing step ([figuring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Figuring "Figuring")), they switched to the custom-built reflective null corrector, designed explicitly to meet very strict tolerances. The incorrect assembly of this device resulted in the mirror being ground very precisely but to the wrong shape. During fabrication, a few tests using conventional null correctors correctly reported [spherical aberration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spherical_aberration "Spherical aberration"). But these results were dismissed, thus missing the opportunity to catch the error, because the reflective null corrector was considered more accurate.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunarWaring1999512-92)

The commission blamed the failings primarily on Perkin-Elmer. Relations between NASA and the optics company had been severely strained during the telescope construction, due to frequent schedule slippage and cost overruns. NASA found that Perkin-Elmer did not review or supervise the mirror construction adequately, did not assign its best optical scientists to the project (as it had for the prototype), and in particular did not involve the optical designers in the construction and verification of the mirror. While the commission heavily criticized Perkin-Elmer for these managerial failings, NASA was also criticized for not picking up on the quality control shortcomings, such as relying totally on test results from a single instrument.[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllenAngelMangusRodney199010-1-93)

### Design of a solution

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=14 "Edit section: Design of a solution")]

[![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Feustel_moving_COSTAR.jpg/250px-Feustel_moving_COSTAR.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Feustel_moving_COSTAR.jpg)

COSTAR being removed by astronaut [Andrew J. Feustel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrew_J._Feustel "Andrew J. Feustel") during SM4 in 2009

Many feared that Hubble would be abandoned.[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTETatarewicz1998374-94) The design of the telescope had always incorporated servicing missions, and astronomers immediately began to seek potential solutions to the problem that could be applied at the first servicing mission, scheduled for 1993. While Kodak had ground a back-up mirror for Hubble, it would have been impossible to replace the mirror in orbit, and too expensive and time-consuming to bring the telescope back to Earth for a refit. Instead, the fact that the mirror had been ground so precisely to the wrong shape led to the design of new optical components with exactly the same error but in the opposite sense, to be added to the telescope at the servicing mission, effectively acting as "[spectacles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glasses "Glasses")" to correct the spherical aberration.[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-95)[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-96)

The first step was a precise characterization of the error in the main mirror. Working backwards from images of point sources, astronomers determined that the [conic constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conic_constant "Conic constant") of the mirror as built was −1.01390±0.0002, instead of the intended −1.00230.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-97)[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-98) The same number was also derived by analyzing the null corrector used by Perkin-Elmer to figure the mirror, as well as by analyzing [interferograms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interferogram "Interferogram") obtained during ground testing of the mirror.[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllenAngelMangusRodney1990E-1-99)

Because of the way the HST's instruments were designed, two different sets of correctors were required. The design of the Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2, already planned to replace the existing WF/PC, included relay mirrors to direct light onto the four separate charge-coupled device (CCD) chips making up its two cameras. An inverse error built into their surfaces could completely cancel the aberration of the primary. However, the other instruments lacked any intermediate surfaces that could be configured in this way, and so required an external correction device.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTETatarewicz1998376-100)

The [Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corrective_Optics_Space_Telescope_Axial_Replacement "Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement") (COSTAR) system was designed to correct the spherical aberration for light focused at the FOC, FOS, and GHRS. It consists of two mirrors in the light path with one ground to correct the aberration.[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-101) To fit the COSTAR system onto the telescope, one of the other instruments had to be removed, and astronomers selected the High Speed Photometer to be sacrificed.[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTETatarewicz1998376-100) By 2002, all the original instruments requiring COSTAR had been replaced by instruments with their own corrective optics.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-COSTARNotNeeded-102) COSTAR was then removed and returned to Earth in 2009 where it is exhibited at the National Air and Space Museum in Washington, D.C.[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-103) The area previously used by COSTAR is now occupied by the [Cosmic Origins Spectrograph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_Origins_Spectrograph "Cosmic Origins Spectrograph").[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-SM4-104)

Servicing missions and new instruments
--------------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=15 "Edit section: Servicing missions and new instruments")]

![Image 20](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/timeline/mai968aydw03eveau3niscpdb5eve8e.png)

Hubble was designed to accommodate regular servicing and equipment upgrades while in orbit. Instruments and limited life items were designed as [orbital replacement units](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_replacement_unit_(HST) "Orbital replacement unit (HST)").[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-105) Five servicing missions (SM 1, 2, 3A, 3B, and 4) were flown by NASA [Space Shuttles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle "Space Shuttle"), the first in December 1993 and the last in May 2009.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-hubble-timeline-106) Servicing missions were delicate operations that began with maneuvering to intercept the telescope in orbit and carefully retrieving it with the shuttle's [mechanical arm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadarm "Canadarm"). The necessary work was then carried out in multiple tethered [spacewalks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spacewalk "Spacewalk") over a period of four to five days. After a visual inspection of the telescope, astronauts conducted repairs, replaced failed or degraded components, upgraded equipment, and installed new instruments. Once work was completed, the telescope was redeployed, typically after boosting to a higher orbit to address the [orbital decay](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_decay "Orbital decay") caused by atmospheric [drag](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drag_(physics) "Drag (physics)").[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-nytimes20150424-107)

### Servicing Mission 1

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=17 "Edit section: Servicing Mission 1")]

[![Image 21](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Upgrading_Hubble_during_SM1.jpg/250px-Upgrading_Hubble_during_SM1.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Upgrading_Hubble_during_SM1.jpg)

Astronauts Musgrave and Hoffman install corrective optics during SM1.

The first Hubble servicing mission was scheduled for 1993 before the mirror problem was discovered. It assumed greater importance, as the astronauts would need to do extensive work to install corrective optics; failure would have resulted in either abandoning Hubble or accepting its permanent disability. Other components failed before the mission, causing the repair cost to rise to $500 million (not including the cost of the shuttle flight). A successful repair would help demonstrate the viability of building [Space Station Alpha](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Station_Alpha "Space Station Alpha").[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTETatarewicz1998374,_378,_381,_388-108)

[STS-49](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-49 "STS-49") in 1992 demonstrated the difficulty of space work. While its rescue of [Intelsat 603](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intelsat_603 "Intelsat 603") received praise, the astronauts had taken possibly reckless risks in doing so. Neither the rescue nor the unrelated assembly of prototype space station components occurred as the astronauts had trained, causing NASA to reassess planning and training, including for the Hubble repair. The agency assigned to the mission [Story Musgrave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Story_Musgrave "Story Musgrave") (who had worked on satellite repair procedures since 1976) and six other experienced astronauts, including two from STS-49. The first mission director since [Project Apollo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Project_Apollo "Project Apollo")[_[clarification needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Please\_clarify "Wikipedia:Please clarify")_] would coordinate a crew with 16 previous shuttle flights. The astronauts were trained to use about a hundred specialized tools.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTETatarewicz1998380%E2%80%93381,_384%E2%80%93387-109)

Heat had been the problem on prior spacewalks, which occurred in sunlight. Hubble needed to be repaired out of sunlight. Musgrave discovered during vacuum training, seven months before the mission, that spacesuit gloves did not sufficiently protect against the cold of space. After [STS-57](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-57 "STS-57") confirmed the issue in orbit, NASA quickly changed equipment, procedures, and flight plan. Seven total mission simulations occurred before launch, the most thorough preparation in shuttle history. No complete Hubble mockup existed, so the astronauts studied many separate models (including one at the Smithsonian) and mentally combined their varying and contradictory details.[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTETatarewicz1998384%E2%80%93387-110)

Service Mission 1 flew aboard [_Endeavour_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour "Space Shuttle Endeavour") in December 1993, and involved installation of several instruments and other equipment over ten days. Most importantly, the [High Speed Photometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High_Speed_Photometer "High Speed Photometer") was replaced with the [COSTAR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corrective_Optics_Space_Telescope_Axial_Replacement "Corrective Optics Space Telescope Axial Replacement") corrective optics package, and WF/PC was replaced with the [Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_Field_and_Planetary_Camera_2 "Wide Field and Planetary Camera 2") (WFPC2) with an internal optical correction system. The [solar arrays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photovoltaic_module "Photovoltaic module") and their drive electronics were also replaced, as well as four gyroscopes in the telescope pointing system, two electrical control units and other electrical components, and two magnetometers. The onboard computers were upgraded with added [coprocessors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coprocessor "Coprocessor"), and Hubble's orbit was boosted.[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Servicing_Mission_1-85)

On January 13, 1994, NASA declared the mission a complete success and showed the first sharper images.[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-111) The mission was one of the most complex performed to that date, involving five long [extra-vehicular activity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity "Extra-vehicular activity") periods. Its success was a boon for NASA, as well as for the astronomers who now had a more capable space telescope.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-:2-77)[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-112)

### Servicing Mission 2

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=18 "Edit section: Servicing Mission 2")]

[![Image 22](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Hubble_Space_Telescope_%2827946391011%29.jpg/250px-Hubble_Space_Telescope_%2827946391011%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hubble_Space_Telescope_(27946391011).jpg)

Hubble, as seen from _Discovery_ during its second servicing mission

Servicing Mission 2, flown by _Discovery_ in February 1997, replaced the GHRS and the FOS with the [Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Telescope_Imaging_Spectrograph "Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph") (STIS) and the [Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near_Infrared_Camera_and_Multi-Object_Spectrometer "Near Infrared Camera and Multi-Object Spectrometer") (NICMOS), replaced an Engineering and Science Tape Recorder with a new Solid State Recorder, and repaired thermal insulation.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-113) NICMOS contained a [heat sink](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat_sink "Heat sink") of solid [nitrogen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nitrogen "Nitrogen") to reduce the [thermal noise](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_noise "Thermal noise") from the instrument, but shortly after it was installed, an unexpected [thermal expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_expansion "Thermal expansion") resulted in part of the heat sink coming into contact with an optical baffle. This led to an increased warming rate for the instrument and reduced its original expected lifetime of 4.5 years to about two years.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-NICMOStemp-114)

### Servicing Mission 3A

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=19 "Edit section: Servicing Mission 3A")]

Servicing Mission 3A, flown by _Discovery_, took place in December 1999, and was a split-off from Servicing Mission 3 after three of the six onboard gyroscopes had failed. The fourth failed a few weeks before the mission, rendering the telescope incapable of performing scientific observations. The mission replaced all six [gyroscopes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gyroscope "Gyroscope"), replaced a Fine Guidance Sensor and the computer, installed a Voltage/temperature Improvement Kit (VIK) to prevent battery overcharging, and replaced thermal insulation blankets.[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-115)

### Servicing Mission 3B

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=20 "Edit section: Servicing Mission 3B")]

Servicing Mission 3B flown by _Columbia_ in March 2002 saw the installation of a new instrument, with the FOC (which, except for the Fine Guidance Sensors when used for astrometry, was the last of the original instruments) being replaced by the [Advanced Camera for Surveys](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Advanced_Camera_for_Surveys "Advanced Camera for Surveys") (ACS). This meant COSTAR was no longer required, since all new instruments had built-in correction for the main mirror aberration.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-COSTARNotNeeded-102) The mission also revived NICMOS by installing a closed-cycle cooler[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-NICMOStemp-114) and replaced the solar arrays for the second time, providing 30 percent more power.[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-116)

### Servicing Mission 4

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=21 "Edit section: Servicing Mission 4")]

[![Image 23](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/STS-125_May_17_EVA.jpg/250px-STS-125_May_17_EVA.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:STS-125_May_17_EVA.jpg)

Hubble during Servicing Mission 4

[![Image 24](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Hubble_telescope_2009.jpg/250px-Hubble_telescope_2009.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hubble_telescope_2009.jpg)

Hubble after release

Plans called for Hubble to be serviced in February 2005, but the [_Columbia_ disaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Columbia_disaster "Columbia disaster") in 2003, in which the orbiter disintegrated on re-entry into the atmosphere, had wide-ranging effects to the Hubble program and other NASA missions. NASA Administrator [Sean O'Keefe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sean_O%27Keefe "Sean O'Keefe") decided all future shuttle missions had to be able to reach the safe haven of the [International Space Station](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Space_Station "International Space Station") should in-flight problems develop. As no shuttles were capable of reaching both HST and the space station during the same mission, future crewed service missions were canceled.[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-117) This decision was criticized by numerous astronomers who felt Hubble was valuable enough to merit the human risk.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-NAS2005-118) HST's planned successor, the James Webb Space Telescope (JWST), by 2004 was not expected to launch until at least 2011. JWST was eventually launched in December 2021.[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-AS-20211225-119) A gap in space-observing capabilities between a decommissioning of Hubble and the commissioning of a successor was of major concern to many astronomers, given the significant scientific impact of HST.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-120) The consideration that JWST will not be located in low Earth orbit, and therefore cannot be easily upgraded or repaired in the event of an early failure, only made concerns more acute. On the other hand, NASA officials were concerned that continuing to service Hubble would consume funds from other programs and delay the JWST.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-121)

In January 2004, O'Keefe said he would review his decision to cancel the final servicing mission to HST, due to public outcry and requests from Congress for NASA to look for a way to save it. The National Academy of Sciences convened an official panel, which recommended in July 2004 that the HST should be preserved despite the apparent risks. Their report urged "NASA should take no actions that would preclude a space shuttle servicing mission to the Hubble Space Telescope".[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Leary-122) In August 2004, O'Keefe asked Goddard Space Flight Center to prepare a detailed proposal for a robotic service mission. These plans were later canceled, the robotic mission being described as "not feasible".[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-123) In late 2004, several Congressional members, led by Senator [Barbara Mikulski](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barbara_Mikulski "Barbara Mikulski"), held public hearings and carried on a fight with much public support (including thousands of letters from school children across the U.S.) to get the Bush Administration and NASA to reconsider the decision to drop plans for a Hubble rescue mission.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-124)

[![Image 25](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/218582main_Batt_FS_img1_lg.jpg/250px-218582main_Batt_FS_img1_lg.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:218582main_Batt_FS_img1_lg.jpg)

Nickel–hydrogen battery pack for Hubble

The nomination in April 2005 of a new NASA Administrator, [Michael D. Griffin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michael_D._Griffin "Michael D. Griffin"), changed the situation, as Griffin stated he would consider a crewed servicing mission.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Green_Light-125) Soon after his appointment Griffin authorized Goddard to proceed with preparations for a crewed Hubble maintenance flight, saying he would make the final decision after the next two shuttle missions. In October 2006 Griffin gave the final go-ahead, and the 11-day mission by _Atlantis_ was scheduled for October 2008.
