Hubble's main data-handling unit failed in September 2008,[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-suddenly_quiet-126) halting all reporting of scientific data until its back-up was brought online on October 25, 2008.[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-127) Since a failure of the backup unit would leave the HST helpless, the service mission was postponed to incorporate a replacement for the primary unit.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-suddenly_quiet-126)

Servicing Mission 4 (SM4), flown by _Atlantis_ in May 2009, was the last scheduled shuttle mission for HST.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-SM4-104)[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-May09-128) SM4 installed the replacement data-handling unit, repaired the ACS and STIS systems, installed improved [nickel–hydrogen batteries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93hydrogen_battery "Nickel–hydrogen battery"), and replaced other components including all six gyroscopes. SM4 also installed two new observation instruments: Wide Field Camera 3 (WFC3) and the [Cosmic Origins Spectrograph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_Origins_Spectrograph "Cosmic Origins Spectrograph") (COS)[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-HST_Opens-129) and the [Soft Capture and Rendezvous System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soft_Capture_and_Rendezvous_System "Soft Capture and Rendezvous System"), which will enable the future rendezvous, capture, and safe disposal of Hubble by either a crewed or robotic mission.[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Soft_Capture-130) Except for the ACS's [High Resolution Channel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Advanced_Camera_for_Surveys#High-Resolution_Channel_(HRC) "Advanced Camera for Surveys"), which could not be repaired and was disabled,[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-131)[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-132)[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-133) the work accomplished during SM4 rendered the telescope fully functional.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-SM4-104)

[![Image 26](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Pillars_of_creation_2014_HST_WFC3-UVIS_full-res_denoised.jpg/250px-Pillars_of_creation_2014_HST_WFC3-UVIS_full-res_denoised.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pillars_of_creation_2014_HST_WFC3-UVIS_full-res_denoised.jpg)

One of Hubble's most famous images, _[Pillars of Creation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pillars\_of\_Creation "Pillars of Creation")_, shows stars forming in the [Eagle Nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eagle_Nebula "Eagle Nebula").

Since the start of the program, a number of research projects have been carried out, some of them almost solely with Hubble, others coordinated facilities such as [Chandra X-ray Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chandra_X-ray_Observatory "Chandra X-ray Observatory") and [ESO](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ESO "ESO")'s [Very Large Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Very_Large_Telescope "Very Large Telescope"). Although the Hubble observatory is nearing the end of its life, there are still major projects scheduled for it. One example is the current (2022) ULLYSES project (Ultraviolet Legacy Library of Young Stars as Essential Standards) which will last for three years to observe a set of high- and low-mass young stars and will shed light on star formation and composition. Another is the OPAL project (Outer Planet Atmospheres Legacy), which is focussed on understanding the evolution and dynamics of the atmosphere of the outer planets (such as Jupiter and Uranus) by making baseline observations over an extended period.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-134)

In an August 2013 press release, [CANDELS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CANDELS "CANDELS") was referred to as "the largest project in the history of Hubble". The survey "aims to explore galactic evolution in the early Universe, and the first seeds of cosmic structure at less than one billion years after the Big Bang."[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-135) The CANDELS project site describes the survey's goals as the following:[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-136)

> The Cosmic Assembly Near-IR Deep Extragalactic Legacy Survey is designed to document the first third of galactic evolution from z = 8 to 1.5 via deep imaging of more than 250,000 galaxies with WFC3/IR and ACS. It will also find the first Type Ia SNe beyond z > 1.5 and establish their accuracy as standard candles for cosmology. Five premier multi-wavelength sky regions are selected; each has multi-wavelength data from Spitzer and other facilities, and has extensive spectroscopy of the brighter galaxies. The use of five widely separated fields mitigates cosmic variance and yields statistically robust and complete samples of galaxies down to 10 9 solar masses out to z ~ 8.

### Frontier Fields program

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Frontier Fields program")]

[![Image 27: Color image of galaxy cluster MCS J0416.1–2403, studied by the Hubble Frontier Fields program](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Color_image_of_galaxy_cluster_MCS_J0416.1%E2%80%932403.jpg/250px-Color_image_of_galaxy_cluster_MCS_J0416.1%E2%80%932403.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Color_image_of_galaxy_cluster_MCS_J0416.1%E2%80%932403.jpg)

The Frontier Fields program studied [MACS0416.1-2403](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MACS0416.1-2403 "MACS0416.1-2403").

The program, officially named "Hubble Deep Fields Initiative 2012", is aimed to advance the knowledge of early [galaxy formation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy_formation "Galaxy formation") by studying high-redshift galaxies in [blank fields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_field "Deep field") with the help of [gravitational lensing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_lensing "Gravitational lensing") to see the "faintest galaxies in the distant universe".[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-SWGreport-137) The Frontier Fields web page describes the goals of the program being:

*   to reveal hitherto inaccessible populations of z = 5–10 galaxies that are ten to fifty times fainter intrinsically than any presently known
*   to solidify our understanding of the stellar masses and star formation histories of sub-L* galaxies at the earliest times
*   to provide the first statistically meaningful morphological characterization of star forming galaxies at z > 5
*   to find z > 8 galaxies stretched out enough by cluster lensing to discern internal structure and/or magnified enough by cluster lensing for spectroscopic follow-up.[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-138)

### Cosmic Evolution Survey (COSMOS)

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Cosmic Evolution Survey (COSMOS)")]

The [Cosmic Evolution Survey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_Evolution_Survey "Cosmic Evolution Survey") (COSMOS)[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Cosmic_Evolution_Survey_COSMOS-139) is an astronomical survey designed to probe the formation and evolution of galaxies as a function of both cosmic time (redshift) and the local galaxy environment. The survey covers a two square degree equatorial field with spectroscopy and X-ray to radio imaging by most of the major space-based telescopes and a number of large ground based telescopes,[[140]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-140) making it a key focus region of extragalactic astrophysics. COSMOS was launched in 2006 as the largest project pursued by the Hubble Space Telescope at the time, and still is the largest continuous area of sky covered for the purposes of mapping deep space in [blank fields](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_field "Deep field"), 2.5 times the area of the moon on the sky and 17 times larger than the largest of the CANDELS regions. The COSMOS scientific collaboration that was forged from the initial COSMOS survey is the largest and longest-running extragalactic collaboration, known for its collegiality and openness. The study of galaxies in their environment can be done only with large areas of the sky, larger than a half square degree.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-141) More than two million galaxies are detected, spanning 90% of the age of the [Universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe "Universe"). The COSMOS collaboration is led by [Caitlin Casey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Caitlin_Casey "Caitlin Casey"), [Jeyhan Kartaltepe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jeyhan_Kartaltepe "Jeyhan Kartaltepe"), and Vernesa Smolcic and involves more than 200 scientists in a dozen countries.[[139]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Cosmic_Evolution_Survey_COSMOS-139)

### Cluster Lensing and Supernova survey with Hubble

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Cluster Lensing and Supernova survey with Hubble")]

The [Cluster Lensing and Supernova survey with Hubble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cluster_Lensing_and_Supernova_survey_with_Hubble "Cluster Lensing and Supernova survey with Hubble") (CLASH) was a Treasury Program from 2010 to 2013 conducted by the Hubble Space Telescope to observe 25 massive galaxy clusters by using gravitational lensing. CLASH aimed to examine the distribution of dark matter and dark energy in massive galaxy clusters with the new instruments installed in 2009. Imagery showing the effects of gravitational lensing is one means of detecting dark matter and dark energy, and CLASH focused on trying to gain a better understanding of both mysterious topics.[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-142)

### Photomosaic of the Andromeda Galaxy

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Photomosaic of the Andromeda Galaxy")]

[![Image 28](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Largest_Mosaic_of_Andromeda_by_Hubble_jan_16_2025-extra_details.jpg/250px-Largest_Mosaic_of_Andromeda_by_Hubble_jan_16_2025-extra_details.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Largest_Mosaic_of_Andromeda_by_Hubble_jan_16_2025-extra_details.jpg)

Largest Mosaic of Andromeda by Hubble with details: (a) Clusters of bright blue stars embedded within the galaxy, background galaxies seen much farther away, and photo-bombing by a couple bright foreground stars that are actually inside our Milky Way; (b) NGC 206 the most conspicuous star cloud in Andromeda; (c) A young cluster of blue newborn stars; (d) The satellite galaxy M32, that may be the residual core of a galaxy that once collided with Andromeda; (e) Dark dust lanes across myriad stars.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-143)

The [Panchromatic Hubble Andromeda Treasury and Panchromatic Hubble Andromeda Southern Treasury](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zooming_In_on_the_Andromeda_Galaxy "Zooming In on the Andromeda Galaxy") (PHAT and PHAST) were observations done by Hubble from July 2010 to October 2013 to map the northern half of the [Andromeda Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andromeda_Galaxy "Andromeda Galaxy") and from December 2021 to January 2024 to map the southern half. Andromeda is the nearest large galaxy to the Milky Way Galaxy and Hubble created the highest resolution and most detailed [photomosaic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photographic_mosaic "Photographic mosaic") ever of Andromeda. Two hundred million stars can be seen in this combined image of both Treasury Programs out of a total one trillion stars in Andromeda. Each star looks like a grain of sand. The northern half, PHAT, was mapped in near-ultraviolet, visible, and near-infrared wavelengths in 828 orbits and was released in January 2015. The southern half, PHAST, was mapped in near-ultraviolet and visible wavelengths in 195 orbits and was released in January 2025. Observing Andromeda in this detail is the best alternative to observing the Milky Way Galaxy because Earth is within the Milky Way and cannot observe most of the Milky Way due to the [galaxy itself blocking observations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zone_of_Avoidance "Zone of Avoidance") of 20% of the sky and most of the galaxy. To achieve this mosaic 1,023 Hubble orbits were needed. The mosaic image is made up of at least 2.5 billion pixels.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-144)[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-145)[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-146)[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-147)

[![Image 29](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/EmissionNebula_NGC6357.jpg/250px-EmissionNebula_NGC6357.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:EmissionNebula_NGC6357.jpg)

Star cluster [Pismis 24](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pismis_24 "Pismis 24") with [nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NGC_6357 "NGC 6357")

Anyone can apply for time on the telescope; there are no restrictions on nationality or academic affiliation, but funding for analysis is available only to U.S. institutions.[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrolgerRose201711-148) Competition for time on the telescope is intense, with about one-fifth of the proposals submitted in each cycle earning time on the schedule.[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-149)[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Telemetry-150)

Calls for proposals are issued roughly annually, with time allocated for a cycle lasting about one year. Proposals are divided into several categories; "general observer" proposals are the most common, covering routine observations. "Snapshot observations" are those in which targets require only 45 minutes or less of telescope time, including overheads such as acquiring the target. Snapshot observations are used to fill in gaps in the telescope schedule that cannot be filled by regular general observer programs.[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrolgerRose201721-151)

Astronomers may make "Target of Opportunity" proposals, in which observations are scheduled if a transient event covered by the proposal occurs during the scheduling cycle. In addition, up to 10% of the telescope time is designated "director's discretionary" (DD) time. Astronomers can apply to use DD time at any time of year, and it is typically awarded for study of unexpected transient phenomena such as supernovae.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrolgerRose201737-152)

Other uses of DD time have included the observations that led to views of the Hubble Deep Field and Hubble Ultra Deep Field, and in the first four cycles of telescope time, observations that were carried out by amateur astronomers.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Omeara1997-153)[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-motherboard20150424-154)

In 2012, the ESA held a contest for public image processing of Hubble data to encourage the discovery of "hidden treasures" in the raw Hubble data.[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-155)[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-156)

### Use by amateur astronomers

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Use by amateur astronomers")]

[![Image 30](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Hubble_Space_Telescope_in_Orion.jpg/250px-Hubble_Space_Telescope_in_Orion.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hubble_Space_Telescope_in_Orion.jpg)

The HST is sometimes visible from the ground, as in this 39-second exposure when it is in Orion. Maximum brightness is about magnitude 1.

The first director of STScI, [Riccardo Giacconi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Riccardo_Giacconi "Riccardo Giacconi"), announced in 1986 that he intended to devote some of his director discretionary time to allowing amateur astronomers to use the telescope. The total time to be allocated was only a few hours per cycle but excited great interest among amateur astronomers.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Omeara1997-153)[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-motherboard20150424-154)

Proposals for amateur time were stringently reviewed by a committee of amateur astronomers, and time was awarded only to proposals that were deemed to have genuine scientific merit, did not duplicate proposals made by professionals, and required the unique capabilities of the space telescope. Thirteen amateur astronomers were awarded time on the telescope, with observations being carried out between 1990 and 1997.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Omeara1997-153) One such study was "[Transition Comets– UV Search for OH](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_search_for_transition_comets "Hubble search for transition comets")". The first proposal, "A Hubble Space Telescope Study of Posteclipse Brightening and Albedo Changes on Io", was published in _Icarus_,[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-157) a journal devoted to solar system studies. A second study from another group of amateurs was also published in _Icarus_.[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-158) After that time, however, budget reductions at STScI made the support of work by amateur astronomers untenable, and no additional amateur programs have been carried out.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Omeara1997-153)[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-motherboard20150424-154)

Regular Hubble proposals still include findings or discovered objects by amateurs and [citizen scientists](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Citizen_science "Citizen science"). These observations are often in a collaboration with professional astronomers. One of the earliest such observations is the [Great White Spot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_White_Spot "Great White Spot") of 1990[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-159) on planet Saturn, discovered by amateur astronomer S. Wilber[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-160) and observed by HST under a proposal by J. Westphal ([Caltech](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology "California Institute of Technology")).[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-161)[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-162) Later professional-amateur observations by Hubble include discoveries by the [Galaxy Zoo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy_Zoo "Galaxy Zoo") project, such as [Voorwerpjes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hanny%27s_Voorwerp "Hanny's Voorwerp") and [Green Pea galaxies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pea_galaxy "Pea galaxy").[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-163)[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-164) The "Gems of the Galaxies" program is based on a list of objects by [Galaxy Zoo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galaxy_Zoo "Galaxy Zoo") volunteers that was shortened with the help of an online vote.[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-165) Additionally there are observations of [minor planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minor_planet "Minor planet") discovered by amateur astronomers, such as [2I/Borisov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2I/Borisov "2I/Borisov") and changes in the atmosphere of the [gas giants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gas_giant "Gas giant") Jupiter and Saturn or the [ice giants](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ice_giant "Ice giant") Uranus and Neptune.[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-166)[[167]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-167) In the pro-am collaboration [backyard worlds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Backyard_Worlds "Backyard Worlds") the HST was used to observe a [planetary mass object](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rogue_planet "Rogue planet"), called [WISE J0830+2837](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/WISE_J0830%2B2837 "WISE J0830+2837"). The non-detection by the HST helped to classify this peculiar object.[[168]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-168)

In the early 1980s, NASA and STScI convened four panels to discuss key projects. These were projects that were both scientifically important and would require significant telescope time, which would be explicitly dedicated to each project. This guaranteed that these particular projects would be completed early, in case the telescope failed sooner than expected. The panels identified three such projects: 1) a study of the nearby intergalactic medium using quasar [absorption lines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absorption_line "Absorption line") to determine the properties of the [intergalactic medium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intergalactic_medium "Intergalactic medium") and the gaseous content of galaxies and groups of galaxies;[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-169) 2) a medium deep survey using the Wide Field Camera to take data whenever one of the other instruments was being used[[170]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-170) and 3) a project to determine the [Hubble constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_constant "Hubble constant") within ten percent by reducing the errors, both external and internal, in the calibration of the distance scale.[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-171)

### Important discoveries

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: Important discoveries")]

[![Image 31](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png/500px-Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_%28full_resolution%29.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field_(full_resolution).png)

Hubble Extreme Deep Field image of space in the constellation [Fornax](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fornax "Fornax")

Hubble has helped resolve some long-standing problems in astronomy, while also raising new questions. Some results have required new [theories](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_theory "Scientific theory") to explain them.

#### Age and expansion of the universe

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=34 "Edit section: Age and expansion of the universe")]

Among its primary mission targets was to measure distances to [Cepheid variable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cepheid_variable "Cepheid variable") stars more accurately than ever before, and thus [constrain the value](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble%27s_law#Using_Hubble_space_telescope_data "Hubble's law") of the [Hubble constant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_constant "Hubble constant"), the measure of the rate at which the universe is expanding, which is also related to its age. Before the launch of HST, estimates of the Hubble constant typically had [errors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Errors_and_residuals_in_statistics "Errors and residuals in statistics") of up to 50%, but Hubble measurements of Cepheid variables in the [Virgo Cluster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Virgo_Cluster "Virgo Cluster") and other distant galaxy clusters provided a measured value with an accuracy of ±10%, which is consistent with other more accurate measurements made since Hubble's launch using other techniques.[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-172) The estimated age of the universe is now 13.7 billion years. (Before the Hubble Telescope it was ten to twenty billion.)[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-173)

Astronomers from the [High-z Supernova Search Team](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High-z_Supernova_Search_Team "High-z Supernova Search Team") and the [Supernova Cosmology Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernova_Cosmology_Project "Supernova Cosmology Project") used ground-based telescopes and HST to observe distant [supernovae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supernova "Supernova") and uncovered evidence that, far from decelerating under the influence of [gravity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity "Gravity"), the expansion of the universe is instead [accelerating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deceleration_parameter "Deceleration parameter"). Three members of these two groups have subsequently been awarded [Nobel Prizes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nobel_Prize "Nobel Prize") for their discovery.[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-174) The cause of this acceleration remains poorly understood;[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-175) the term used for the currently-unknown cause is [dark energy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_energy "Dark energy"), signifying that it is dark (unable to be directly seen and detected) to our current scientific instruments.[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-176)

The high-resolution spectra and images provided by the HST have been especially well-suited to establishing the prevalence of [black holes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_hole "Black hole") in the center of nearby galaxies. While it had been hypothesized in the early 1960s that black holes would be found at the centers of some galaxies, and astronomers in the 1980s identified a number of good black hole candidates, work conducted with Hubble shows that black holes are probably common to the centers of all galaxies.[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-177) The Hubble programs further established that the masses of the nuclear black holes and properties of the galaxies are closely related.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-178)[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-179)

#### Extending visible wavelength images

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: Extending visible wavelength images")]

A unique window on the Universe enabled by Hubble are the [Hubble Deep Field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Deep_Field "Hubble Deep Field"), [Hubble Ultra-Deep Field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Ultra-Deep_Field "Hubble Ultra-Deep Field"), and [Hubble Extreme Deep Field](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Extreme_Deep_Field "Hubble Extreme Deep Field") images, which used Hubble's unmatched sensitivity at visible wavelengths to create images of small patches of sky that are the deepest ever obtained at optical wavelengths. The images reveal galaxies billions of light years away, thereby providing information about the early Universe, and have accordingly generated a wealth of scientific papers. The Wide Field Camera 3 improved the view of these fields in the infrared and ultraviolet, supporting the discovery of some of the most distant objects yet discovered, such as [MACS0647-JD](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MACS0647-JD "MACS0647-JD").[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-heic-180)

The non-standard object [SCP 06F6](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SCP_06F6 "SCP 06F6") was discovered by the Hubble Space Telescope in February 2006.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-nature-181)[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-gans-182)

On March 3, 2016, researchers using Hubble data announced the discovery of the farthest confirmed galaxy to date: [GN-z11](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GN-z11 "GN-z11"), which Hubble observed as it existed roughly 400 million years after the Big Bang.[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-GN-z11-183) The Hubble observations occurred on February 11, 2015, and April 3, 2015, as part of the [CANDELS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CANDELS "CANDELS")/[GOODS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/GOODS "GOODS")-North surveys.[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-184)[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-185)

#### Solar System discoveries

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Solar System discoveries")]

[![Image 32](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Saturn.Aurora.HST.UV-Vis.jpg/250px-Saturn.Aurora.HST.UV-Vis.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Saturn.Aurora.HST.UV-Vis.jpg)

Hubble's STIS UV and ACS visible light combined to reveal Saturn's southern aurora

[![Image 33](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg/250px-Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Jupiter_showing_SL9_impact_sites.jpg)

Brown spots mark [Comet Shoemaker–Levy 9](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker%E2%80%93Levy_9 "Comet Shoemaker–Levy 9") impact sites on [Jupiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jupiter "Jupiter")'s southern hemisphere

The collision of [Comet Shoemaker-Levy 9](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker-Levy_9 "Comet Shoemaker-Levy 9") with [Jupiter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jupiter "Jupiter") in 1994 was fortuitously timed for astronomers, coming just a few months after Servicing Mission 1 had restored Hubble's optical performance. Hubble images of the [planet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planet "Planet") were sharper than any taken since the passage of _[Voyager 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voyager\_2 "Voyager 2")_ in 1979, and were crucial in studying the dynamics of the collision of a large comet with Jupiter, an event believed to occur once every few centuries.[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-186)

In March 2015, researchers announced that measurements of aurorae around [Ganymede](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ganymede_(moon) "Ganymede (moon)"), one of Jupiter's moons, revealed that it has a subsurface ocean. Using Hubble to study the motion of its aurorae, the researchers determined that a large saltwater ocean was helping to suppress the interaction between Jupiter's magnetic field and that of Ganymede. The ocean is estimated to be 100 km (60 mi) deep, trapped beneath a 150 km (90 mi) ice crust.[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-187)[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-search_ocean_Ganymede-188)

HST has also been used to study objects in the outer reaches of the Solar System, including the dwarf planets [Pluto](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pluto "Pluto"),[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-189)[Eris](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet) "Eris (dwarf planet)"),[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-190) and [Sedna](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/90377_Sedna "90377 Sedna").[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-191) During June and July 2012, U.S. astronomers using Hubble discovered [Styx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Styx_(moon) "Styx (moon)"), a tiny fifth moon orbiting Pluto.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-IAUCirc-192)

From June to August 2015, Hubble was used to [search](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Horizons#KBO_Search "New Horizons") for a [Kuiper belt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuiper_belt "Kuiper belt") object (KBO) target for the _[New Horizons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New\_Horizons "New Horizons")_ Kuiper Belt Extended Mission (KEM) when similar searches with ground telescopes failed to find a suitable target.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-193) This resulted in the discovery of at least five new KBOs, including the eventual KEM target, [486958 Arrokoth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/486958_Arrokoth "486958 Arrokoth"), that _New Horizons_ performed a close fly-by of on January 1, 2019.[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-NASA-20141015-194)[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-195)[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-196)

In April 2022 NASA announced that astronomers were able to use images from HST to determine the size of the nucleus of comet [C/2014 UN271 (Bernardinelli–Bernstein)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/C/2014_UN271_(Bernardinelli%E2%80%93Bernstein) "C/2014 UN271 (Bernardinelli–Bernstein)"), which is the largest icy comet nucleus ever seen by astronomers. The nucleus of C/2014 UN271 has an estimated mass of fifty trillion tons which is fifty times the mass of other known comets in the Solar System.[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-197)

[![Image 34](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Hubble_and_ALMA_image_of_MACS_J1149.5%2B2223.jpg/250px-Hubble_and_ALMA_image_of_MACS_J1149.5%2B2223.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hubble_and_ALMA_image_of_MACS_J1149.5%2B2223.jpg)

Hubble and ALMA image of [MACS J1149.5+2223](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MACS_J1149.5%2B2223 "MACS J1149.5+2223")[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-198)

#### Supernova reappearance

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=38 "Edit section: Supernova reappearance")]

On December 11, 2015, Hubble captured an image of the first-ever predicted reappearance of a supernova, dubbed "[Refsdal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SN_Refsdal "SN Refsdal")", which was calculated using different mass models of a galaxy cluster whose gravity is [warping](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_relativity "General relativity") the supernova's light. The supernova was previously seen in November 2014 behind galaxy cluster [MACS J1149.5+2223](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MACS_J1149.5%2B2223 "MACS J1149.5+2223") as part of Hubble's Frontier Fields program. The light from the cluster took roughly five billion years to reach Earth, while the light from the supernova behind it took five billion more years than that, as measured by their respective [redshifts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Redshift "Redshift"). Because of the gravitational effect of the galaxy cluster, four images of the supernova appeared instead of one, an example of an [Einstein cross](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Einstein_Cross "Einstein Cross"). Based on early lens models, a fifth image was predicted to reappear by the end of 2015.[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-199) Refsdal reappeared as predicted in 2015.[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-200)

#### Mass and size of Milky Way

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=39 "Edit section: Mass and size of Milky Way")]

In March 2019, observations from Hubble and data from the European Space Agency's [Gaia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaia_(spacecraft) "Gaia (spacecraft)") space observatory were combined to determine that the mass of the [Milky Way Galaxy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way_Galaxy "Milky Way Galaxy") is approximately 1.5 trillion times the mass of the Sun, a value intermediate between prior estimates.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-201)

Other discoveries made with Hubble data include proto-planetary disks ([proplyds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proplyd "Proplyd")) in the [Orion Nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_Nebula "Orion Nebula");[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-202) evidence for the presence of [extrasolar planets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extrasolar_planet "Extrasolar planet") around Sun-like stars;[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-203) and the optical counterparts of the still-mysterious [gamma-ray bursts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gamma-ray_burst "Gamma-ray burst").[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-204) Using [gravitational lensing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_lens "Gravitational lens"), Hubble observed a galaxy designated [MACS 2129-1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MACS_2129-1 "MACS 2129-1") approximately ten billion lightyears from Earth. MACS 2129-1 subverted expectations about galaxies in which new star formation had ceased, a significant result for understanding the formation of [elliptical galaxies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elliptical_galaxy "Elliptical galaxy").[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-205)

In 2022 Hubble detected the light of the farthest individual star ever seen to date. The star, [WHL0137-LS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/WHL0137-LS "WHL0137-LS") (nicknamed _Earendel_), existed within the first billion years after the [Big Bang](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Bang "Big Bang"). It will be observed by NASA's James Webb Space Telescope to confirm Earendel is indeed a star.[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-206)

### Impact on astronomy

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=41 "Edit section: Impact on astronomy")]

[![Image 35: Depiction of progress in the detection of the early Universe](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Hubble_Probes_the_Early_Universe.jpg/960px-Hubble_Probes_the_Early_Universe.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hubble_Probes_the_Early_Universe.jpg)

Evolution of detecting the [early Universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang "Timeline of the Big Bang")

[![Image 36](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Hs-2009-25-e-full.jpg/330px-Hs-2009-25-e-full.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hs-2009-25-e-full.jpg)

Some of the Carina nebula by WFC3

Many objective measures show the positive impact of Hubble data on astronomy. As of 2025, over 22,000 [papers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scientific_paper "Scientific paper") based on Hubble data have been published in peer-reviewed journals,[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-207) and countless more have appeared in conference [proceedings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proceedings "Proceedings"). Looking at papers several years after their publication, about one-third of all astronomy papers have no [citations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Citation "Citation"), while only two percent of papers based on Hubble data have no citations. On average, a paper based on Hubble data receives about twice as many citations as papers based on non-Hubble data. Of the 200 papers published each year that receive the most citations, about 10% are based on Hubble data.[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-208)

Although the HST has clearly helped astronomical research, its financial cost has been large. A study on the relative astronomical benefits of different sizes of telescopes found that while papers based on HST data generate 15 times as many citations as a 4 m (13 ft) ground-based telescope such as the [William Herschel Telescope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Herschel_Telescope "William Herschel Telescope"), the HST costs about 100 times as much to build and maintain.[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-209)

Deciding between building ground- versus space-based telescopes is complex. Even before Hubble was launched, specialized ground-based techniques such as [aperture masking interferometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aperture_masking_interferometry "Aperture masking interferometry") had obtained higher-resolution optical and infrared images than Hubble would achieve, though restricted to targets about 10 8 times brighter than the faintest targets observed by Hubble.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-210)[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-211) Since then, advances in [adaptive optics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adaptive_optics "Adaptive optics") have extended the high-resolution imaging capabilities of ground-based telescopes to the infrared imaging of faint objects. The usefulness of adaptive optics versus HST observations depends strongly on the particular details of the research questions being asked. In the visible bands, adaptive optics can correct only a relatively small field of view, whereas HST can conduct high-resolution optical imaging over a wider field.[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-:3-212) Moreover, Hubble can image more faint objects, since ground-based telescopes are affected by the background of scattered light created by the Earth's atmosphere.[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-:4-213)

### Impact on aerospace engineering

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=42 "Edit section: Impact on aerospace engineering")]

In addition to its scientific results, Hubble has also made significant contributions to [aerospace engineering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aerospace_engineering "Aerospace engineering"), in particular the performance of systems in low Earth orbit (LEO). These insights result from Hubble's long lifetime on orbit, extensive instrumentation, and return of assemblies to the Earth where they can be studied in detail. In particular, Hubble has contributed to studies of the behavior of [graphite composite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carbon_fiber_reinforced_polymer "Carbon fiber reinforced polymer") structures in vacuum, optical contamination from residual gas and human servicing, [radiation damage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiation_damage "Radiation damage") to electronics and sensors, and the long term behavior of [multi-layer insulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multi-layer_insulation "Multi-layer insulation").[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-214) One lesson learned was that gyroscopes assembled using pressurized oxygen to deliver suspension fluid were prone to failure due to electric wire corrosion. Gyroscopes are now assembled using pressurized nitrogen.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Gyros-215) Another is that optical surfaces in LEO can have surprisingly long lifetimes; Hubble was only expected to last 15 years before the mirror became unusable, but after 14 years there was no measureable degradation.[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-NAS2005-118) Finally, Hubble servicing missions, particularly those that serviced components not designed for in-space maintenance, have contributed towards the development of new tools and techniques for on-orbit repair.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-216)

[![Image 37](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Hubble_stretches_the_stellar_tape_measure_ten_times_further.jpg/500px-Hubble_stretches_the_stellar_tape_measure_ten_times_further.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hubble_stretches_the_stellar_tape_measure_ten_times_further.jpg)

Hubble precision stellar distance measurement has been extended ten times further into the [Milky Way](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milky_Way "Milky Way").[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-217)

### Transmission to Earth

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=44 "Edit section: Transmission to Earth")]

Hubble data was initially stored on the spacecraft. When launched, the storage facilities were old-fashioned reel-to-reel [tape drives](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tape_drive "Tape drive"), but these were replaced by [solid state](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid-state_drive "Solid-state drive") data storage facilities during servicing missions 2 and 3A. About twice daily, the Hubble Space Telescope radios data to a satellite in the [geosynchronous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geosynchronous "Geosynchronous")[Tracking and Data Relay Satellite System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tracking_and_Data_Relay_Satellite_System "Tracking and Data Relay Satellite System") (TDRSS), which then downlinks the science data to one of two 60-foot (18-meter) diameter high-gain microwave antennas located at the [White Sands Test Facility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_Sands_Test_Facility "White Sands Test Facility") in [White Sands, New Mexico](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/White_Sands,_New_Mexico "White Sands, New Mexico").[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Telemetry-150) From there they are sent to the Space Telescope Operations Control Center at Goddard Space Flight Center, and finally to the Space Telescope Science Institute for archiving.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Telemetry-150) Each week, HST downlinks approximately 140 gigabits of data.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-hubblesite-facts-2)

[![Image 38](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Cumulative-absorption-spectrum-hubble-telescope.jpg/500px-Cumulative-absorption-spectrum-hubble-telescope.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Cumulative-absorption-spectrum-hubble-telescope.jpg)

Data analysis of a spectrum revealing the chemistry of hidden clouds

All images from Hubble are [monochromatic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monochrome "Monochrome")[grayscale](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grayscale "Grayscale"), taken through a variety of filters, each passing specific wavelengths of light, and incorporated in each camera. Color images are created by combining separate monochrome images taken through different filters. This process can also create [false-color](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/False-color "False-color") versions of images including infrared and ultraviolet channels, where infrared is typically rendered as a deep red and ultraviolet is rendered as a deep blue.[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-space20130724-218)[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-219)

All Hubble data is eventually made available via the [Mikulski Archive for Space Telescopes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Telescope_Science_Institute "Space Telescope Science Institute") at [STScI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Telescope_Science_Institute "Space Telescope Science Institute"),[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-220)[CADC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian_Astronomy_Data_Centre "Canadian Astronomy Data Centre")[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-221) and [ESA/ESAC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Space_Astronomy_Centre "European Space Astronomy Centre").[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-222) Data is usually proprietary (available only to the [principal investigator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principal_investigator "Principal investigator") (PI) and astronomers designated by the PI) for twelve months after being taken. The PI can apply to the director of the STScI to extend or reduce the proprietary period in some circumstances.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrolgerRose201753-223)

Observations made on Director's Discretionary Time are exempt from the proprietary period, and are released to the public immediately. Calibration data such as flat fields and [dark frames](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dark_frame "Dark frame") are also publicly available straight away. All data in the archive is in the [FITS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/FITS "FITS") format, which is suitable for astronomical analysis but not for public use.[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose201769-224) The [Hubble Heritage Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Heritage_Project "Hubble Heritage Project") processes and releases to the public a small selection of the most striking images in [JPEG](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/JPEG "JPEG") and [TIFF](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tagged_Image_File_Format "Tagged Image File Format") formats.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-heritage.stsci-225)

Astronomical data taken with CCDs must undergo several calibration steps before they are suitable for astronomical analysis. STScI has developed sophisticated software that automatically calibrates data when they are requested from the archive using the best calibration files available. This "on-the-fly" processing means large data requests can take a day or more to be processed and returned. The process by which data is calibrated automatically is known as "pipeline reduction", and is increasingly common at major observatories. Astronomers may if they wish retrieve the calibration files themselves and run the pipeline reduction software locally. This may be desirable when calibration files other than those selected automatically need to be used.[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose201767%E2%80%9369-226)

Hubble data can be analyzed using many different packages. STScI maintains the custom-made [Space Telescope Science Data Analysis System](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Telescope_Science_Data_Analysis_System "Space Telescope Science Data Analysis System") (STSDAS) software, which contains all the programs needed to run pipeline reduction on raw data files, as well as many other astronomical image processing tools, tailored to the requirements of Hubble data. The software runs as a module of [IRAF](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IRAF "IRAF"), a popular astronomical data reduction program.[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-FOOTNOTERose201768%E2%80%9369-227)

Outreach activities
-------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=49 "Edit section: Outreach activities")]

[![Image 39](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Horsehead-Hubble.jpg/250px-Horsehead-Hubble.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Horsehead-Hubble.jpg)

In 2001, NASA polled internet users to find out what they would most like Hubble to observe; they overwhelmingly selected the [Horsehead Nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horsehead_Nebula "Horsehead Nebula").

[![Image 40](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Hubble_Space_Telescope_Scale_Model.JPG/250px-Hubble_Space_Telescope_Scale_Model.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hubble_Space_Telescope_Scale_Model.JPG)

One-quarter scale model at the courthouse in [Marshfield, Missouri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marshfield,_Missouri "Marshfield, Missouri"), a hometown of Edwin Hubble

NASA considered it important for the Space Telescope to capture the public's imagination, given the considerable contribution of [taxpayers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taxpayer "Taxpayer") to its construction and operational costs.[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-228) After the difficult early years when the faulty mirror severely dented Hubble's reputation with the public, the first servicing mission allowed its rehabilitation as the corrected optics produced numerous remarkable images.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-:2-77)[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-229)

Several initiatives have helped to keep the public informed about Hubble activities. In the United States, [outreach](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Outreach "Outreach") efforts are coordinated by the Space Telescope Science Institute (STScI) Office for Public Outreach, which was established in 2000 to ensure that U.S. taxpayers saw the benefits of their investment in the space telescope program. To that end, STScI operates the HubbleSite.org website. The [Hubble Heritage Project](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Heritage_Project "Hubble Heritage Project"), operating out of the STScI, provides the public with high-quality images of the most interesting and striking objects observed. The Heritage team is composed of amateur and professional astronomers, as well as people with backgrounds outside astronomy, and emphasizes the [aesthetic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aesthetic "Aesthetic") nature of Hubble images. The Heritage Project is granted a small amount of time to observe objects which, for scientific reasons, may not have images taken at enough wavelengths to construct a full-color image.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-heritage.stsci-225)

Since 1999, the leading Hubble outreach group in Europe has been the [Hubble European Space Agency Information Centre](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_European_Space_Agency_Information_Centre "Hubble European Space Agency Information Centre") (HEIC).[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-230) This office was established at the [Space Telescope European Coordinating Facility](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Telescope_European_Coordinating_Facility "Space Telescope European Coordinating Facility") in Munich, Germany. HEIC's mission is to fulfill HST outreach and education tasks for the European Space Agency. The work is centered on the production of news and photo releases that highlight interesting Hubble results and images. These are often European in origin, and so increase awareness of both ESA's Hubble share (15%) and the contribution of European scientists to the observatory. ESA produces educational material, including a [videocast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Videocast "Videocast") series called Hubblecast designed to share world-class scientific news with the public.[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-231)

The Hubble Space Telescope has won two Space Achievement Awards from the [Space Foundation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Foundation "Space Foundation"), for its outreach activities, in 2001 and 2010.[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-232)

A replica of the Hubble Space Telescope is displayed on the courthouse lawn in [Marshfield, Missouri](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marshfield,_Missouri "Marshfield, Missouri"), the hometown of namesake Edwin P. Hubble.[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-233)

[![Image 41](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/HH_901_and_HH_902_in_the_Carina_nebula_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg/250px-HH_901_and_HH_902_in_the_Carina_nebula_%28captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:HH_901_and_HH_902_in_the_Carina_nebula_(captured_by_the_Hubble_Space_Telescope).jpg)

A pillar of gas and dust in the [Carina Nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carina_Nebula "Carina Nebula"). This [Wide Field Camera 3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wide_Field_Camera_3 "Wide Field Camera 3") image, dubbed _[Mystic Mountain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mystic\_Mountain "Mystic Mountain")_, was released in 2010 to commemorate Hubble's 20th anniversary in space.

The Hubble Space Telescope celebrated its 20th anniversary in space on April 24, 2010. To commemorate the occasion, NASA, ESA, and the Space Telescope Science Institute (STScI) released an image from the [Carina Nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carina_Nebula "Carina Nebula").[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-234)

To commemorate Hubble's 25th anniversary in space on April 24, 2015, STScI released images of the [Westerlund 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Westerlund_2 "Westerlund 2") cluster, located about 20,000 light-years (6,100 pc) away in the constellation Carina, through its Hubble 25 website.[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-235) The European Space Agency created a dedicated 25th anniversary page on its website.[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-236) In April 2016, a special celebratory image of the [Bubble Nebula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bubble_Nebula "Bubble Nebula") was released for Hubble's 26th "birthday".[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-237)

### Gyroscope rotation sensors

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=52 "Edit section: Gyroscope rotation sensors")]

HST uses gyroscopes to detect and measure any rotations so it can stabilize itself in orbit and point accurately and steadily at astronomical targets. HST has six of these rate-sensing gyroscopes installed. Three gyroscopes are normally required for operation; observations are still possible with two or one, but the area of sky that can be viewed would be somewhat restricted, and observations requiring very accurate pointing are more difficult.[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-238) In 2018, the plan was to drop into one-gyroscope mode if fewer than three working gyroscopes were operational. The gyroscopes are part of the **Pointing Control System**, which uses five types of sensors (magnetic sensors, optical sensors, and the gyroscopes) and two types of [actuators](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Actuator "Actuator") ([reaction wheels](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reaction_wheel "Reaction wheel") and [magnetic torquers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_torquer "Magnetic torquer")).[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-PCS-47)

After the [_Columbia_ disaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster "Space Shuttle Columbia disaster") in 2003, it was unclear whether another servicing mission would be possible, and gyroscope life became a concern again, so engineers developed new software for two-gyroscope and one-gyroscope modes to maximize the potential lifetime. The development was successful, and in 2005, it was decided to switch to two-gyroscope mode for regular telescope operations as a means of extending the lifetime of the mission. The switch to this mode was made in August 2005, leaving Hubble with two gyroscopes in use, two on backup, and two inoperable.[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-239) One more gyroscope failed in 2007.[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-240)

By the time of the final repair mission in May 2009, during which all six gyroscopes were replaced (with two new pairs and one refurbished pair), only three were still working. Engineers determined that the gyroscope failures were caused by corrosion of electric wires powering the motor that was initiated by oxygen-pressurized air used to deliver the thick suspending fluid.[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Gyros-215) The new gyroscope models were assembled using pressurized nitrogen[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Gyros-215) and were expected to be much more reliable.[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-241) In the 2009 servicing mission all six gyroscopes were replaced, and after almost ten years only three gyroscopes failed, and only after exceeding the average expected run time for the design.[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-242)

Of the six gyroscopes replaced in 2009, three were of the old design susceptible for flex-lead failure, and three were of the new design with a longer expected lifetime. The first of the old-style gyroscopes failed in March 2014, and the second in April 2018. On October 5, 2018, the last of the old-style gyroscopes failed, and one of the new-style gyroscopes was powered-up from standby state. However, that reserve gyroscope did not immediately perform within operational limits, and so the observatory was placed into "safe" mode while scientists attempted to fix the problem.[[243]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-243)[[244]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-244) NASA tweeted on October 22, 2018, that the "rotation rates produced by the backup gyro have reduced and are now within a normal range. Additional tests [are] to be performed to ensure Hubble can return to science operations with this gyro."[[245]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-245)

The solution that restored the backup new-style gyroscope to operational range was widely reported as "turning it off and on again".[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-246) A "running restart" of the gyroscope was performed, but this had no effect, and the final resolution to the failure was more complex. The failure was attributed to an inconsistency in the fluid surrounding the float within the gyroscope, i.e. an air bubble. On October 18, 2018, the Hubble Operations Team directed the spacecraft into a series of maneuvers, moving it in opposite directions in order to mitigate the inconsistency. Only after the maneuvers, and a subsequent set of maneuvers on October 19, did the gyroscope truly operate within its normal range.[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-247)

### Instruments and electronics

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=53 "Edit section: Instruments and electronics")]

[![Image 42](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3f/Formalhaut_b.jpg/500px-Formalhaut_b.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Formalhaut_b.jpg)

Hubble views the [Fomalhaut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fomalhaut "Fomalhaut") system. This false-color image was taken in October 2004 and July 2006 with the Advanced Camera for Surveys.

Past servicing missions have exchanged old instruments for new ones, avoiding failure and making new types of science possible. Without servicing missions, all the instruments will eventually fail. In August 2004, the power system of the Space Telescope Imaging Spectrograph (STIS) failed, rendering the instrument inoperable. The electronics had originally been fully redundant, but the first set of electronics failed in May 2001.[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-248) This power supply was fixed during Servicing Mission 4 in May 2009.[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-249)

Similarly, the Advanced Camera for Surveys (ACS) main camera primary electronics failed in June 2006, and the power supply for the backup electronics failed on January 27, 2007.[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-250) Only the instrument's Solar Blind Channel (SBC) was operable using the side-1 electronics. A new power supply for the wide angle channel was added during SM 4, but quick tests revealed this did not help the high resolution channel.[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-251) The Wide Field Channel (WFC) was returned to service by STS-125 in May 2009 but the High Resolution Channel (HRC) remains offline.[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-252)

On January 8, 2019, Hubble entered a partial safe mode following suspected hardware problems in its most advanced instrument, the Wide Field Camera 3 instrument. NASA later reported that the cause of the safe mode within the instrument was a detection of voltage levels out of a defined range. On January 15, 2019, NASA said the cause of the failure was a software problem. Engineering data within the telemetry circuits were not accurate. In addition, all other telemetry within those circuits also contained erroneous values indicating that this was a telemetry issue and not a power supply issue. After resetting the telemetry circuits and associated boards the instrument began functioning again. On January 17, 2019, the device was returned to normal operation and on the same day it completed its first science observations.[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-253)[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-254)

#### 2021 power control issue

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=54 "Edit section: 2021 power control issue")]

On June 13, 2021, Hubble's payload computer halted due to a suspected issue with a memory module. An attempt to restart the computer on June 14 failed. Further attempts to switch to one of three other backup memory modules on board the spacecraft failed on June 18. On June 23 and 24, NASA engineers switched Hubble to a backup payload computer, but these operations have failed as well with the same error. On June 28, 2021, NASA announced that it was extending the investigation to other components.[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-255)[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-256) Scientific operations were suspended while NASA worked to diagnose and resolve the issue.[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-AP:_Computer_trouble,_science_halted-257)[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-258) After identifying a malfunctioning power control unit (PCU) supplying power to one of Hubble's computers, NASA was able to switch to a backup PCU and bring Hubble back to operational mode on July 16.[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-259)[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-260)[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-261)[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-262) On October 23, 2021, HST instruments reported missing synchronization messages[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-263) and went into safe mode.[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-264) By December 8, 2021, NASA had restored full science operations and was developing updates to make instruments more resilient to missing synchronization messages.[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-265)

### Orbital decay and controlled reentry

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=56 "Edit section: Orbital decay and controlled reentry")]

[![Image 43](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Soft_Capture_Mechanism_installed_on_Hubble_%28illustration%29.jpg/250px-Soft_Capture_Mechanism_installed_on_Hubble_%28illustration%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Soft_Capture_Mechanism_installed_on_Hubble_(illustration).jpg)

Illustration of the Soft Capture Mechanism (SCM) installed on Hubble

Hubble orbits the Earth in the extremely tenuous upper [atmosphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere "Earth's atmosphere"), and over time its orbit [decays](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbital_decay "Orbital decay") due to [drag](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drag_(physics) "Drag (physics)"). If not [reboosted](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reboost "Reboost"), it will re-enter the Earth's atmosphere within some decades, with the exact date depending on how active the Sun is and its impact on the upper atmosphere. If Hubble were to descend in a completely uncontrolled re-entry, parts of the main mirror and its support structure would probably survive, leaving the potential for damage or even human fatalities.[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-266) In 2013, deputy project manager James Jeletic projected that Hubble could survive into the 2020s.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-cbsnews20130530-4) Based on solar activity and atmospheric drag, or lack thereof, a natural atmospheric reentry for Hubble will occur between 2028 and 2040.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-cbsnews20130530-4)[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-267) In June 2016, NASA extended the service contract for Hubble until June 2021.[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-268) In November 2021, NASA extended the service contract for Hubble until June 2026.[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-269)

NASA's original plan for safely de-orbiting Hubble was to [retrieve it using a Space Shuttle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STS-144 "STS-144"). Hubble would then have most likely been displayed in the [Smithsonian Institution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Smithsonian_Institution "Smithsonian Institution"). This is no longer possible since the [Space Shuttle fleet has been retired](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_Shuttle_retirement "Space Shuttle retirement"), and would have been unlikely in any case due to the cost of the mission and risk to the crew. Instead, NASA considered adding an external propulsion module to allow controlled re-entry.[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-270) Ultimately, in 2009, as part of Servicing Mission 4, the last servicing mission by the Space Shuttle, NASA installed the Soft Capture Mechanism (SCM), to enable deorbit by either a crewed or robotic mission. The SCM, together with the Relative Navigation System (RNS), mounted on the Shuttle to collect data to "enable NASA to pursue numerous options for the safe de-orbit of Hubble", constitute the Soft Capture and Rendezvous System (SCRS).[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Soft_Capture-130)[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-271)

### Possible service missions

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Hubble_Space_Telescope&action=edit&section=57 "Edit section: Possible service missions")]

As of 2017, the [Trump Administration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_presidency_of_Donald_Trump "First presidency of Donald Trump") was considering a proposal by the [Sierra Nevada Corporation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_Corporation "Sierra Nevada Corporation") to use a crewed version of its [Dream Chaser](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dream_Chaser "Dream Chaser") spacecraft to service _Hubble_ some time in the 2020s as a continuation of its scientific capabilities and as insurance against any malfunctions in the James Webb Space Telescope.[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-Popular_Mechanics-2017-02-03-272) In 2020, [John Grunsfeld](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Grunsfeld "John Grunsfeld") said that [SpaceX Crew Dragon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SpaceX_Crew_Dragon "SpaceX Crew Dragon") or [Orion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orion_(spacecraft) "Orion (spacecraft)") could perform another repair mission within ten years. While robotic technology is not yet sophisticated enough, he said, with another crewed visit "We could keep Hubble going for another few decades" with new gyros and instruments.[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-foust20200615-273)

Billionaire [private astronaut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commercial_astronaut "Commercial astronaut")[Jared Isaacman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jared_Isaacman "Jared Isaacman") proposed to fund a servicing mission using SpaceX spacecraft. Though it might save NASA much money, SpaceX and NASA differed on the mission's risk.[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-274) In September 2022, NASA and SpaceX signed a Space Act Agreement to investigate the possibility of launching a Crew Dragon mission to service and boost Hubble to a higher orbit, possibly extending its lifespan by another 20 years.[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-275) This mission could have been the second of the [Polaris Program](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polaris_Program "Polaris Program"), but by June 2024 NASA had rejected a private servicing mission because of potential damage to the observatory.[[276]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-276)[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-277)[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-278)

**[Visible spectrum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Visible_spectrum "Visible spectrum") range**
[Color](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Color "Color")[Wavelength](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wavelength "Wavelength")
**[violet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Violet_(color) "Violet (color)")**380–450 nm
**[blue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blue "Blue")**450–475 nm
**[cyan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyan "Cyan")**476–495 nm
**[green](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green "Green")**495–570 nm
**[yellow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yellow "Yellow")**570–590 nm
**[orange](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orange_(color) "Orange (color)")**590–620 nm
**[red](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Red "Red")**620–750 nm

Design, size, and mirror comparison

There is no direct replacement to Hubble as an ultraviolet and visible light space telescope, because near-term space telescopes do not duplicate Hubble's wavelength coverage (near-ultraviolet to near-infrared wavelengths), instead concentrating on the further infrared bands. These bands are preferred for studying high redshift and low-temperature objects, objects generally older and farther away in the universe. These wavelengths are also difficult or impossible to study from the ground, justifying the expense of a space-based telescope. Large ground-based telescopes can image some of the same wavelengths as Hubble, sometimes challenge HST in terms of resolution by using [adaptive optics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adaptive_optics "Adaptive optics") (AO), have much larger light-gathering power, and can be upgraded more easily, but cannot yet match Hubble's excellent resolution over a wide field of view with the very dark background of space.[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-:3-212)[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-:4-213)

Plans for a Hubble successor materialized as the Next Generation Space Telescope project, which culminated in plans for the James Webb Space Telescope (JWST), the formal successor of Hubble.[[279]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-SAOverview-279) Very different from a scaled-up Hubble, it is designed to operate colder and farther away from the Earth at the L2 [Lagrangian point](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrangian_point "Lagrangian point"), where thermal and optical interference from the Earth and Moon are lessened. It is not engineered to be fully serviceable (such as replaceable instruments), but the design includes a docking ring to enable visits from other spacecraft.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-280) A main scientific goal of JWST is to observe the most distant objects in the universe, beyond the reach of existing instruments. It is expected to detect stars in the [early Universe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang "Timeline of the Big Bang") approximately 280 million years older than stars HST now detects.[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-281) The telescope is an international collaboration between NASA, the European Space Agency, and the [Canadian Space Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency "Canadian Space Agency") since 1996,[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-282) and was launched on December 25, 2021, on an [Ariane 5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ariane_5 "Ariane 5") rocket.[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-283) Although JWST is primarily an infrared instrument, its coverage extends down to 600 nm wavelength light, or roughly orange in the visible spectrum. A typical human eye can see to about 750 nm wavelength light, so there is some overlap with the longest visible wavelength bands, including orange and red light.[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-284)

[![Image 44](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/JWST-HST-primary-mirrors.svg/250px-JWST-HST-primary-mirrors.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:JWST-HST-primary-mirrors.svg)

Hubble and JWST mirrors

(4.0 m 2 and 25m 2 respectively)

A complementary telescope, looking at even longer wavelengths than Hubble or JWST, was the European Space Agency's [Herschel Space Observatory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory "Herschel Space Observatory"), launched on May 14, 2009. Like JWST, Herschel was not designed to be serviced after launch, and had a mirror substantially larger than Hubble's, but observed only in the [far infrared](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Far_infrared "Far infrared") and [submillimeter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Submillimeter_astronomy "Submillimeter astronomy"). It needed helium coolant, of which it ran out on April 29, 2013.[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-bbc-285)

**Selected space telescopes and instruments**[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope#cite_note-286)
Name Year Wavelength Aperture
Human eye—0.39–0.75 μm 0.005 m
[Spitzer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope "Spitzer Space Telescope")2003 3–180 μm 0.85 m
Hubble STIS 1997 0.115–1.03 μm 2.4 m
Hubble WFC3 2009 0.2–1.7 μm 2.4 m
[Herschel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory "Herschel Space Observatory")2009 55–672 μm 3.5 m
[JWST](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Webb_Space_Telescope "James Webb Space Telescope")2021 0.6–28.5 μm 6.5 m
